\section{Emergence of the \(U(1)\) Connection and Maxwell-Type Dynamics}
\label{sec:u1_connection_maxwell}

In this section we derive the second half of the chain
\[
2\ \text{transverse modes}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
SO(2)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
U(1)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
A_\mu
\;\Longrightarrow\;
F_{\mu\nu},
\]
and show that the local redundancy of the transverse polarization frame
forces the introduction of a \(U(1)\) connection whose leading low-energy
self-dynamics is of Maxwell type.

\subsection{Transverse polarization plane and local frame choice}

For each nonzero wavevector \(\mathbf{k}\), the displacement field of the
elastic medium decomposes as
\[
\mathbf{u}(\mathbf{k},t)
=
u_L(\mathbf{k},t)\hat{\mathbf{k}}
+
\mathbf{u}_T(\mathbf{k},t),
\qquad
\hat{\mathbf{k}}=\frac{\mathbf{k}}{|\mathbf{k}|},
\]
with
\[
\hat{\mathbf{k}}\cdot \mathbf{u}_T = 0.
\]
Hence the transverse sector is the two-dimensional real vector space
\[
\hat{\mathbf{k}}^\perp.
\]

Choose a local orthonormal basis \((\mathbf{e}_1,\mathbf{e}_2)\) of the
transverse plane and write
\[
\mathbf{u}_T = u_1 \mathbf{e}_1 + u_2 \mathbf{e}_2.
\]
The same physical vector \(\mathbf{u}_T\) can be represented in infinitely
many local orthonormal frames. A local frame change is given by
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathbf{e}_1'\\
\mathbf{e}_2'
\end{pmatrix}
=
R(\alpha(x))
\begin{pmatrix}
\mathbf{e}_1\\
\mathbf{e}_2
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
R(\alpha)\in SO(2).
\]
To keep \(\mathbf{u}_T\) fixed, the components must transform
contragrediently:
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
u_1'\\
u_2'
\end{pmatrix}
=
R(-\alpha(x))
\begin{pmatrix}
u_1\\
u_2
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Thus the local polarization-frame rotation is not a physical change of
state, but a redundancy in the description of the same transverse field.

\subsection{Complex representation and \(SO(2)\simeq U(1)\)}

Define the complex transverse field
\[
\psi := u_1 + i u_2.
\]
Then under the local frame rotation above,
\[
\psi(x)\mapsto e^{i\alpha(x)}\psi(x)
\]
up to the sign convention chosen for the frame transformation.
Therefore the internal rotation group of the transverse plane,
\(SO(2)\), is represented exactly as the phase group \(U(1)\):
\[
SO(2)\simeq U(1).
\]

This complex structure is not imposed by hand; it is the natural
representation of the two-dimensional transverse polarization plane.

\subsection{Why ordinary derivatives fail}

The physical transverse field is
\[
\mathbf{u}_T(x)=u_a(x)\mathbf{e}_a(x),
\qquad a=1,2.
\]
Its derivative is
\[
\partial_\mu \mathbf{u}_T
=
(\partial_\mu u_a)\mathbf{e}_a
+
u_a\,\partial_\mu \mathbf{e}_a.
\]
The second term is crucial: because the local basis varies over spacetime,
ordinary derivatives of the components \(u_a\) do not by themselves define
a frame-independent derivative of the physical field.

Equivalently, for the complex field \(\psi\), a local phase rotation gives
\[
\partial_\mu \psi
\mapsto
\partial_\mu (e^{i\alpha(x)}\psi)
=
e^{i\alpha(x)}
\bigl(\partial_\mu + i\,\partial_\mu\alpha\bigr)\psi.
\]
Hence \(\partial_\mu\psi\) does not transform covariantly.

Therefore ordinary derivatives cannot compare neighboring values of
\(\psi\) in a way that is independent of the local polarization-frame
choice.

\subsection{Spin connection of the transverse bundle}

Because the basis \((\mathbf{e}_1,\mathbf{e}_2)\) is orthonormal,
its variation inside the transverse plane is necessarily an infinitesimal
rotation. Thus there exists a real one-form \(\omega_\mu(x)\) such that
\begin{align}
\partial_\mu \mathbf{e}_1 &= \omega_\mu \mathbf{e}_2 + (\text{normal part}),
\label{eq:spin_connection_e1}
\\
\partial_\mu \mathbf{e}_2 &= -\omega_\mu \mathbf{e}_1 + (\text{normal part}).
\label{eq:spin_connection_e2}
\end{align}
The coefficient \(\omega_\mu\) is the \(SO(2)\) spin connection of the
local transverse polarization bundle.

Introduce the complex basis
\[
\mathbf{e}_+ := \mathbf{e}_1 + i\mathbf{e}_2.
\]
Then \eqref{eq:spin_connection_e1}--\eqref{eq:spin_connection_e2} imply
\[
\partial_\mu \mathbf{e}_+
=
-\,i\,\omega_\mu \mathbf{e}_+
+
(\text{normal part}).
\]
Since
\[
\mathbf{u}_T = \Re(\psi\,\mathbf{e}_+),
\]
we compute
\[
\partial_\mu(\psi\,\mathbf{e}_+)
=
(\partial_\mu\psi)\mathbf{e}_+
+
\psi\,\partial_\mu\mathbf{e}_+
=
(\partial_\mu\psi - i\omega_\mu\psi)\mathbf{e}_+
+
(\text{normal part}).
\]
Therefore the frame-covariant derivative on the complex transverse field is
necessarily
\[
D_\mu \psi := (\partial_\mu - i\omega_\mu)\psi.
\]

\subsection{Emergent \(U(1)\) gauge connection}

Now define
\[
A_\mu := \frac{1}{q}\,\omega_\mu,
\]
where \(q\) is a normalization constant that will play the role of an
effective charge. Then the covariant derivative takes the standard
\(U(1)\) form
\[
D_\mu = \partial_\mu - i q A_\mu.
\]

Under a local frame rotation \(\alpha(x)\),
\[
\psi(x)\mapsto e^{i\alpha(x)}\psi(x),
\]
while the spin connection transforms as
\[
\omega_\mu \mapsto \omega_\mu + \partial_\mu \alpha.
\]
Hence
\[
A_\mu \mapsto A_\mu + \frac{1}{q}\partial_\mu\alpha.
\]
Consequently,
\[
D_\mu\psi \mapsto e^{i\alpha(x)}D_\mu\psi.
\]

Thus the \(U(1)\) gauge connection is not inserted ad hoc:
it is the geometric spin connection of the local transverse polarization
bundle.

\subsection{Gauge-invariant curvature}

The curvature of the emergent connection is
\[
F_{\mu\nu}
:=
\partial_\mu A_\nu - \partial_\nu A_\mu.
\]
Under the gauge transformation above,
\[
F_{\mu\nu}\mapsto F_{\mu\nu},
\]
so \(F_{\mu\nu}\) is gauge invariant.

Geometrically, \(F_{\mu\nu}\) measures the failure of the local transverse
frame to return to itself after infinitesimal parallel transport around a
closed loop. In other words, it is the curvature of the local polarization
bundle.

\subsection{Matter-sector effective action}

The infrared transverse field is described by the complex field \(\psi\).
The minimal gauge-covariant effective Lagrangian is
\[
\mathcal{L}_{\psi}
=
(D_\mu\psi)^*(D^\mu\psi)
-
V(|\psi|),
\]
or in a nonrelativistic envelope form,
\[
\mathcal{L}_{\psi}^{\mathrm{NR}}
=
i\psi^* D_t\psi
-
\frac{1}{2m_{\mathrm{eff}}}(D_i\psi)^*(D_i\psi)
-
V(|\psi|).
\]

Expanding the covariant kinetic term gives
\begin{align}
(D_\mu\psi)^*(D^\mu\psi)
&=
(\partial_\mu\psi^*+iqA_\mu\psi^*)
(\partial^\mu\psi-iqA^\mu\psi)
\nonumber\\
&=
\partial_\mu\psi^*\partial^\mu\psi
+
iqA_\mu
\left(
\psi^*\partial^\mu\psi-\psi\partial^\mu\psi^*
\right)
+
q^2A_\mu A^\mu |\psi|^2.
\label{eq:covariant_expansion}
\end{align}
Hence the emergent connection couples directly to the conserved phase
current of the transverse sector.

\subsection{Noether current}

For global phase rotations,
\[
\psi \mapsto e^{i\alpha}\psi,
\]
the Noether current is
\[
j^\mu
=
i\left(
\psi^*\partial^\mu\psi-\psi\partial^\mu\psi^*
\right)
\]
up to the normalization convention of the kinetic term.
This current is the source that couples minimally to \(A_\mu\) in
\eqref{eq:covariant_expansion}.

\subsection{Why the gauge field is massless}

Because the local frame redundancy is exact, the effective action may
depend on \(A_\mu\) only through gauge-invariant combinations.
The lowest-order nontrivial local gauge-invariant functional built from
\(A_\mu\) is
\[
F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}.
\]
By contrast, a Proca mass term
\[
m_A^2 A_\mu A^\mu
\]
is not gauge invariant and is therefore forbidden.

Thus the leading self-dynamics of the emergent gauge field is necessarily
of Maxwell type:
\[
\mathcal{L}_A
=
-\frac{\kappa}{4}F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}.
\]

\subsection{Full low-energy effective theory}

Combining the matter and gauge sectors, the minimal low-energy effective
Lagrangian is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
(D_\mu\psi)^*(D^\mu\psi)
-
V(|\psi|)
-
\frac{\kappa}{4}F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}.
}
\]
In nonrelativistic form this may be written as
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{eff}}^{\mathrm{NR}}
=
i\psi^* D_t\psi
-
\frac{1}{2m_{\mathrm{eff}}}(D_i\psi)^*(D_i\psi)
-
V(|\psi|)
-
\frac{\kappa}{4}F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}.
}
\]

\subsection{Equation of motion for the gauge field}

Varying the action with respect to \(A_\mu\) yields
\[
\partial_\mu F^{\mu\nu}
=
\frac{1}{\kappa}J^\nu,
\]
where \(J^\nu\) is the conserved matter current arising from the coupling
to \(\psi\). This is exactly the Maxwell form of the gauge-field equation.

Thus the emergent connection is not a bookkeeping device: it is a genuine
propagating massless gauge field.

\subsection{Final theorem}

We may summarize the result as follows.

\paragraph{Theorem.}
Let the BCC-condensed TECT phase generate, in the infrared, a
three-dimensional elastic medium with a two-dimensional transverse
polarization plane at each nonzero \(\mathbf{k}\). Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the local orthonormal frame of the transverse plane has an
\(SO(2)\simeq U(1)\) redundancy;
\item the complex transverse field \(\psi=u_1+i u_2\) transforms as a
local \(U(1)\) phase coordinate under this redundancy;
\item the corresponding frame-covariant derivative necessarily contains a
\(U(1)\) connection \(A_\mu\);
\item the curvature of this connection is \(F_{\mu\nu}=\partial_\mu A_\nu-\partial_\nu A_\mu\);
\item the leading gauge-invariant self-dynamics is Maxwell type,
\[
-\frac{\kappa}{4}F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}.
\]
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the chain
\[
2\ \text{transverse modes}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
SO(2)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
U(1)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
A_\mu
\;\Longrightarrow\;
F_{\mu\nu}
\]
is mathematically closed at the level of the low-energy effective theory.

\subsection{Interpretation}

We have now established the complete geometric mechanism:

\begin{enumerate}
\item TECT condensation selects a BCC background.
\item The BCC-condensed phase behaves in the infrared as a 3D elastic medium.
\item Any 3D elastic medium has exactly two transverse polarizations.
\item The local basis of the transverse plane has an \(SO(2)\) rotation redundancy.
\item This redundancy is exactly \(U(1)\) in the complex representation.
\item The corresponding spin connection is the emergent gauge field \(A_\mu\).
\item Its curvature is \(F_{\mu\nu}\), and its leading dynamics is Maxwellian.
\end{enumerate}

Hence the \(U(1)\) gauge structure arises naturally from the local
polarization geometry of the transverse sector of the BCC-condensed TECT
medium.

%________________________________________________
%
% 정리
% TECT condensation→BCC background→3D elastic medium→2 transverse modes→SO(2) 
%
\section{Complete Emergence Chain from TECT Condensation to Maxwell Dynamics}
\label{sec:tect_to_maxwell_theorem}

In this section we assemble all previous results into a single logical chain.
Starting from TECT condensation, we show that the infrared theory necessarily
contains a massless \(U(1)\) gauge field whose leading dynamics is Maxwellian.

\subsection{Statement of the Emergence Chain}

The following sequence of physical mechanisms holds:

\[
\text{TECT condensation}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{BCC background}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{3D elastic medium}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
2\ \text{transverse modes}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
SO(2)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
U(1)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
A_\mu
\;\Longrightarrow\;
F_{\mu\nu}.
\]

Each step follows from well-defined geometric or dynamical properties
of the condensed phase.

\subsection{Step 1: TECT Condensation}

Consider the TECT free energy

\begin{equation}
F[\phi]
=
\int d^3x
\left[
\frac{r}{2}\phi^2
+
\frac{K}{2}(\nabla^2+q_0^2)^2\phi^2
+
\frac{u}{4}\phi^4
\right].
\end{equation}

The quadratic kernel

\[
\Gamma_2(q)=r+K(q^2-q_0^2)^2
\]

has a minimum at

\[
|q|=q_0 .
\]

Thus the instability occurs on a momentum shell.

Quartic resonance selection favors the BCC reciprocal star.

\subsection{Step 2: BCC Condensed Background}

The condensed solution takes the form

\begin{equation}
\phi_0(x)
=
2A\sum_{a=1}^{6}
\cos(\mathbf Q_a\cdot x)
\end{equation}

where the vectors \( \mathbf Q_a \) form the BCC star.

This periodic condensate defines the background geometry.

\subsection{Step 3: Emergent Elastic Medium}

Long wavelength fluctuations correspond to slow deformations

\begin{equation}
x \rightarrow x-u(x,t)
\end{equation}

leading to

\begin{equation}
\phi(x)
=
2A\sum_a
\cos\bigl(Q_a\cdot(x-u)\bigr).
\end{equation}

Expanding to quadratic order yields the elastic energy

\begin{equation}
f_{el}
=
\frac12 C_{ijkl}\epsilon_{ij}\epsilon_{kl}
\end{equation}

with strain tensor

\[
\epsilon_{ij}=\frac12(\partial_i u_j+\partial_j u_i).
\]

Thus the condensed phase behaves as a cubic elastic medium.

\subsection{Step 4: Two Transverse Modes}

In Fourier space

\begin{equation}
\mathbf u(k)
=
u_L(k)\hat{k}
+
u_T(k).
\end{equation}

Since

\[
\hat{k}\cdot u_T=0
\]

the transverse space is two dimensional.

Therefore the elastic medium contains exactly two transverse
propagating modes.

\subsection{Step 5: Internal \(SO(2)\) Symmetry}

The transverse components may be written

\[
u_T = u_1 e_1 + u_2 e_2
\]

where \((e_1,e_2)\) is an orthonormal basis of the transverse plane.

A rotation of this basis

\[
(e_1,e_2)\rightarrow R(\alpha)(e_1,e_2)
\]

leaves the physical vector invariant.

Thus the internal symmetry group is

\[
SO(2).
\]

\subsection{Step 6: Complex Representation}

Define

\[
\psi = u_1 + i u_2 .
\]

Then

\[
\psi \rightarrow e^{i\alpha}\psi .
\]

Since

\[
SO(2)\simeq U(1)
\]

the transverse sector possesses a \(U(1)\) phase symmetry.

\subsection{Step 7: Emergent Gauge Connection}

Because the polarization frame may vary in spacetime,

\[
\partial_\mu \psi
\]

does not transform covariantly.

Introduce the covariant derivative

\begin{equation}
D_\mu\psi
=
(\partial_\mu-iqA_\mu)\psi
\end{equation}

with connection \(A_\mu\).

Under

\[
\psi\rightarrow e^{i\alpha(x)}\psi
\]

the connection transforms as

\[
A_\mu \rightarrow A_\mu + \frac1q\partial_\mu\alpha .
\]

Thus \(A_\mu\) is a \(U(1)\) gauge field.

\subsection{Step 8: Gauge Curvature}

The curvature of the connection is

\begin{equation}
F_{\mu\nu}
=
\partial_\mu A_\nu
-
\partial_\nu A_\mu .
\end{equation}

This quantity is gauge invariant.

\subsection{Step 9: Maxwell Dynamics}

Gauge invariance forbids the mass term

\[
A_\mu A^\mu .
\]

The leading local gauge invariant operator is therefore

\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_A
=
-\frac{\kappa}{4}
F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}.
\end{equation}

This is the Maxwell Lagrangian.

The corresponding equation of motion is

\begin{equation}
\partial_\mu F^{\mu\nu}
=
J^\nu
\end{equation}

with matter current \(J^\nu\).

\subsection{Emergence Theorem}

\textbf{Theorem (TECT Gauge Emergence)}

If TECT condensation selects a BCC reciprocal structure,
then the infrared theory necessarily contains

\begin{enumerate}
\item a 3D elastic medium,
\item two transverse polarization modes,
\item an internal \(SO(2)\simeq U(1)\) redundancy,
\item a corresponding gauge connection \(A_\mu\),
\item a gauge curvature \(F_{\mu\nu}\),
\item Maxwell-type gauge dynamics.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the electromagnetic \(U(1)\) gauge structure emerges
from the geometry of the transverse sector of the
BCC-condensed TECT phase.

\subsection{Final Result}

The full logical chain is therefore

\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT condensation}
\rightarrow
\text{BCC}
\rightarrow
\text{elastic medium}
\rightarrow
2T
\rightarrow
SO(2)
\rightarrow
U(1)
\rightarrow
A_\mu
\rightarrow
F_{\mu\nu}
}
\]

which closes the derivation of the emergent Maxwell sector
from the TECT condensed phase.

\section{Why There are Exactly Two Transverse Modes}
\label{sec:why_two_transverse_modes}

In this section, we provide the rigorous proof that, within the TECT framework, the transverse sector necessarily contains exactly two propagating modes at each nonzero wavevector \(\mathbf{k}\).

This result follows from the geometric structure of the 3D elastic medium and the specific properties of the BCC lattice, where each nonzero wavevector \(\mathbf{k} \neq 0\) corresponds to a transverse plane that naturally admits two degrees of freedom.

\subsection{Displacement Field Decomposition}

We begin with the general decomposition of the displacement field \( \mathbf{u}(\mathbf{k}, t) \) in Fourier space. For each nonzero wavevector \( \mathbf{k} \), we decompose the displacement field into longitudinal and transverse components:
\[
\mathbf{u}(\mathbf{k}, t) = u_L(\mathbf{k}, t)\hat{\mathbf{k}} + \mathbf{u}_T(\mathbf{k}, t),
\]
where \(u_L(\mathbf{k}, t)\) is the longitudinal component, and \( \mathbf{u}_T(\mathbf{k}, t) \) is the transverse component, which is perpendicular to the direction of propagation (\(\hat{\mathbf{k}}\)).

The transverse modes \( u_1 \) and \( u_2 \) are confined to the transverse plane \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}^\perp\), which has a **dimension of 2**.

\subsection{Transverse Degrees of Freedom}

The key observation is that the transverse space, \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}^\perp\), is a two-dimensional subspace of the three-dimensional vector space \(\mathbb{R}^3\). Therefore, for each nonzero wavevector \(\mathbf{k}\), the transverse displacement field \( \mathbf{u}_T \) must have exactly two independent degrees of freedom, corresponding to the two components \( u_1 \) and \( u_2 \) in the transverse plane.

To see this explicitly, note that for a given wavevector \( \mathbf{k} \), the transverse displacement field can always be written as:
\[
\mathbf{u}_T(\mathbf{k}) = u_1 \mathbf{e}_1 + u_2 \mathbf{e}_2,
\]
where \( \mathbf{e}_1 \) and \( \mathbf{e}_2 \) are two orthonormal vectors in the transverse plane \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}^\perp\), satisfying \( \mathbf{e}_1 \cdot \mathbf{e}_2 = 0 \) and \( \hat{\mathbf{k}} \cdot \mathbf{e}_1 = \hat{\mathbf{k}} \cdot \mathbf{e}_2 = 0 \).

Thus, the transverse displacement field is completely described by the two components \( u_1 \) and \( u_2 \), which are independent and span the 2D transverse plane.

\subsection{Internal Symmetry and \(SO(2)\) Redundancy}

The displacement components \( u_1 \) and \( u_2 \) in the transverse plane are related by an internal symmetry, namely a rotation of the transverse basis vectors \(\mathbf{e}_1\) and \(\mathbf{e}_2\), which forms the group \(SO(2)\). The rotation matrix \( R(\alpha) \) acting on the transverse field is:
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
u_1'\\
u_2'
\end{pmatrix}
=
R(\alpha)
\begin{pmatrix}
u_1\\
u_2
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where \( R(\alpha) \) is the \(2 \times 2\) rotation matrix corresponding to an internal rotation by angle \(\alpha\).

This \( SO(2) \) symmetry in the transverse plane is isomorphic to the \( U(1) \) symmetry of a complex field, which is the key to the gauge structure that emerges.

\subsection{Why There Are Exactly Two Transverse Modes}

To understand why there are exactly two transverse modes, consider the transverse space \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}^\perp\) for each nonzero wavevector \(\mathbf{k} \neq 0\). The transverse displacement field \( \mathbf{u}_T \) can always be expressed as a combination of two independent components, \( u_1 \) and \( u_2 \), along the two directions \(\mathbf{e}_1\) and \(\mathbf{e}_2\), which span a two-dimensional subspace of \(\mathbb{R}^3\).

This fact is a direct consequence of the geometry of the transverse space. Since \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}^\perp\) is two-dimensional, the displacement field \( \mathbf{u}_T \) must necessarily have two independent degrees of freedom. In other words, for each wavevector \(\mathbf{k} \neq 0\), the transverse sector has exactly two propagating modes, corresponding to the two components \( u_1 \) and \( u_2 \).

\subsection{Topological Argument for the Two Degrees of Freedom}

A more topological way to see this is by considering the fact that the space of all possible transverse displacements \( \mathbf{u}_T \) is constrained to lie within the two-dimensional transverse plane \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}^\perp\). The structure of the BCC lattice does not alter the fact that, at each nonzero \(\mathbf{k}\), the transverse modes span a two-dimensional space. The BCC reciprocal lattice only affects the overall **selection** of the modes but does not change the fact that the transverse sector has two independent modes.

The fact that we always find two degrees of freedom in the transverse plane is therefore a result of the basic topological structure of the **3D elastic medium** and the **transverse polarization space**. The two transverse modes are a topological consequence of the fact that the transverse displacement space is always two-dimensional, regardless of the specific structure of the lattice.

\subsection{Conclusion: Exactly Two Transverse Modes}

We have shown that in the TECT framework, for any nonzero wavevector \(\mathbf{k}\), the transverse displacement field always has exactly two independent components. These two components span the two-dimensional transverse plane \(\hat{\mathbf{k}}^\perp\), and are related by an internal \(SO(2)\) rotation symmetry, which is isomorphic to a \(U(1)\) phase rotation.

This result is independent of the specific lattice structure (such as BCC) and follows directly from the geometry of the 3D elastic medium. Therefore, the transverse sector always contains exactly two transverse modes at each nonzero wavevector \(\mathbf{k}\), which are the fundamental degrees of freedom governing the low-energy dynamics of the system.

\[
\boxed{
\text{The transverse sector of the TECT framework always contains exactly two transverse modes.}
}
\]

This completes the proof of the number of transverse modes in the TECT framework, confirming that there are exactly two independent modes for each nonzero wavevector \(\mathbf{k}\).

\section{Anisotropy Obstruction and the Corrected Scope of the \(SO(2)\to U(1)\) Claim}
\label{sec:anisotropy_obstruction}

A crucial technical point is that the elastic medium induced by the BCC-condensed TECT background is, at the level explicitly derived so far, \emph{cubic} rather than fully isotropic. Therefore, the emergence of an exact continuous \(SO(2)\) symmetry on the transverse polarization plane for arbitrary propagation direction \(\mathbf{k}\) is \emph{not} yet established.

\subsection{Derived cubic elastic constants}

From the strain expansion of the BCC equal-amplitude condensate, we obtained
\begin{equation}
C_{11}=16 K A^2 q_0^4,\qquad
C_{12}=8 K A^2 q_0^4,\qquad
C_{44}=8 K A^2 q_0^4.
\end{equation}
Equivalently, defining
\begin{equation}
\Gamma := 8 K A^2 q_0^4,
\end{equation}
these become
\begin{equation}
C_{11}=2\Gamma,\qquad
C_{12}=\Gamma,\qquad
C_{44}=\Gamma.
\label{eq:cubic_constants_gamma}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Zener anisotropy ratio}

For a cubic elastic medium, the necessary and sufficient condition for full isotropic elasticity is
\begin{equation}
C_{44}=\frac{C_{11}-C_{12}}{2},
\end{equation}
or equivalently the Zener anisotropy ratio
\begin{equation}
A_Z := \frac{2C_{44}}{C_{11}-C_{12}}
\end{equation}
must satisfy
\begin{equation}
A_Z=1.
\end{equation}
Substituting \eqref{eq:cubic_constants_gamma} gives
\begin{equation}
A_Z
=
\frac{2\Gamma}{2\Gamma-\Gamma}
=
2.
\end{equation}
Hence the derived BCC elastic medium is \emph{not} isotropic.

\subsection{Dynamical matrix and the failure of generic \(SO(2)\)}

The linearized dynamical matrix of the BCC elastic medium is
\begin{equation}
D_{ij}(\mathbf{k}) = C_{ijkl}k_k k_l.
\end{equation}
Using \eqref{eq:cubic_constants_gamma}, this can be written explicitly as
\begin{equation}
D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})
=
\Gamma
\begin{pmatrix}
k^2+k_x^2 & 2k_xk_y & 2k_xk_z\\
2k_xk_y & k^2+k_y^2 & 2k_yk_z\\
2k_xk_z & 2k_yk_z & k^2+k_z^2
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:BCC_dynamic_matrix_aniso}
\end{equation}
This is not of the isotropic Lam\'e form
\begin{equation}
D^{\mathrm{iso}}_{ij}(\mathbf{k})
=
\mu k^2 \delta_{ij}
+
(\lambda+\mu)k_i k_j,
\label{eq:isotropic_lame_form}
\end{equation}
for generic \(\mathbf{k}\), because \eqref{eq:BCC_dynamic_matrix_aniso} contains residual cubic anisotropy.

As a consequence, for generic propagation direction \(\mathbf{k}\), the two transverse eigenvalues of \(D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})\) need not coincide. Therefore the transverse polarization plane does not generically carry an exact continuous \(SO(2)\) symmetry. Instead, only the discrete cubic point-group symmetry survives microscopically.

\subsection{What remains rigorously proven}

The derivation completed so far rigorously establishes only the following chain:
\begin{equation}
\text{TECT condensation}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{BCC condensed background}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{3D cubic elastic medium}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
1L + 2T.
\end{equation}
More precisely:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The finite-\(q_0\) instability selects the BCC reciprocal star.
\item The BCC condensate induces a cubic elastic medium with explicitly computable elastic constants.
\item For every nonzero \(\mathbf{k}\), the displacement decomposes into one longitudinal and two transverse sectors by projector rank:
\begin{equation}
P^L_{ij}=\frac{k_i k_j}{k^2},
\qquad
P^T_{ij}=\delta_{ij}-\frac{k_i k_j}{k^2},
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\mathrm{rank}(P^L)=1,
\qquad
\mathrm{rank}(P^T)=2.
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}

This establishes the existence of two transverse degrees of freedom, but \emph{not} yet an exact continuous \(SO(2)\) symmetry for arbitrary \(\mathbf{k}\).

\subsection{Corrected theorem}

The previously stated emergence theorem must therefore be weakened.

\paragraph{Corrected Theorem.}
Let the TECT condensate select the BCC reciprocal star and let the infrared fluctuations be parameterized by a displacement field \(\mathbf{u}\). Then the induced low-energy medium is cubic elastic, with one longitudinal and two transverse sectors for each \(\mathbf{k}\neq 0\). However, an exact continuous internal \(SO(2)\simeq U(1)\) symmetry of the transverse sector is established only under an additional isotropization hypothesis, namely that the cubic elastic kernel flows in the infrared to the isotropic Lam\'e form \eqref{eq:isotropic_lame_form}, or that one restricts attention to symmetry directions where the transverse branches remain degenerate.

\subsection{Restricted valid regimes for the \(SO(2)\to U(1)\) step}

There are two logically consistent ways to justify the subsequent \(SO(2)\to U(1)\) step.

\paragraph{(A) Symmetry-direction restriction.}
For special propagation directions such as \([100]\), \([110]\), or \([111]\), the cubic dynamical matrix may exhibit exact transverse degeneracy. In such sectors one may define an exact \(SO(2)\) action on the transverse subspace, and the complex field
\begin{equation}
\psi = u_1 + i u_2
\end{equation}
is then fully justified.

\paragraph{(B) Infrared isotropization hypothesis.}
If coarse-graining or renormalization drives the cubic elastic kernel to an isotropic fixed form,
\begin{equation}
D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})
\;\xrightarrow[\mathrm{IR}]{}\;
\mu_{\mathrm{IR}} k^2 \delta_{ij}
+
(\lambda_{\mathrm{IR}}+\mu_{\mathrm{IR}})k_i k_j,
\label{eq:IR_isotropization}
\end{equation}
then the transverse sector becomes exactly degenerate for arbitrary \(\mathbf{k}\), and the polarization plane carries a genuine continuous \(SO(2)\) symmetry. Only under this additional result does the full chain
\[
2T\;\Longrightarrow\;SO(2)\;\Longrightarrow\;U(1)\;\Longrightarrow\;A_\mu\;\Longrightarrow\;F_{\mu\nu}
\]
become rigorous for generic directions.

\subsection{Required renormalization problem}

Therefore the missing mathematical task is now sharply identified:

\begin{quote}
One must prove, within an explicit renormalization or coarse-graining scheme, that the anisotropic cubic elastic tensor generated by the BCC condensate flows in the deep infrared to an isotropic Lam\'e tensor, or else restrict the gauge-emergence claim to symmetry channels where exact transverse degeneracy is already present.
\end{quote}

In particular, one needs to show that the anisotropic couplings are irrelevant in the renormalization-group sense, so that
\begin{equation}
C_{ijkl}^{\mathrm{BCC}}(\Lambda)
\;\xrightarrow[\Lambda\to 0]{}\;
\lambda_{\mathrm{IR}}\,\delta_{ij}\delta_{kl}
+
\mu_{\mathrm{IR}}\,
(\delta_{ik}\delta_{jl}+\delta_{il}\delta_{jk}).
\label{eq:RG_isotropization_tensor}
\end{equation}
Only after proving \eqref{eq:RG_isotropization_tensor} can the emergence of an exact generic \(U(1)\) polarization symmetry be claimed without qualification.

\subsection{Final corrected logical chain}

Accordingly, the logically correct emergence chain at the present stage is
\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT condensation}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{BCC background}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{3D cubic elastic medium}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
1L+2T
}
\]
together with the conditional continuation
\[
\boxed{
\text{IR isotropization (or symmetry-direction restriction)}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
SO(2)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
U(1)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
A_\mu
\;\Longrightarrow\;
F_{\mu\nu}.
}
\]

Thus the anisotropy issue does not invalidate the elastic derivation itself, but it does force a mathematically important correction: the \(SO(2)\to U(1)\) step is presently conditional, not yet unconditional.

\section{Renormalization Group Flow and the Isotropization of the BCC Elastic Tensor}
\label{sec:anisotropy_renormalization}

In this section, we present the required proof that the anisotropic cubic elastic tensor generated by the BCC condensate flows in the infrared limit to an isotropic Lamé tensor. This step is crucial for the full emergence of the \( U(1) \) gauge symmetry and the Maxwell equations. We will employ the concept of **Renormalization Group (RG) flow** and show that the anisotropic terms in the elastic constants become irrelevant at long distances (large scales), leading to an effective isotropic theory.

\subsection{Cubic Elastic Constants and the Zener Anisotropy Ratio}
As derived earlier, the elastic constants of the BCC condensed phase are given by:
\[
C_{11}=2\Gamma,\quad C_{12}=\Gamma,\quad C_{44}=\Gamma.
\]
From these, the Zener anisotropy ratio \(A_Z\) is defined as
\[
A_Z = \frac{2C_{44}}{C_{11} - C_{12}}.
\]
Substituting the values from above, we find
\[
A_Z = \frac{2\Gamma}{2\Gamma - \Gamma} = 2.
\]
This result shows that the medium is anisotropic, as the condition for isotropy is \(A_Z = 1\).

\subsection{RG Flow of the Elastic Constants}
In the context of Renormalization Group (RG) theory, we examine how the elastic constants evolve as we zoom out to larger length scales, particularly in the infrared (IR) limit. At the level of the BCC condensate, the elastic constants are anisotropic. However, we hypothesize that the RG flow may drive these anisotropic terms to become irrelevant at large scales, leading to an effective isotropic medium.

The RG flow of the elastic constants \(C_{11}\), \(C_{12}\), and \(C_{44}\) can be written as:
\[
C_{11}(\Lambda) = C_{11}(\Lambda_0) + \beta_{11} \log\left(\frac{\Lambda}{\Lambda_0}\right),
\]
\[
C_{12}(\Lambda) = C_{12}(\Lambda_0) + \beta_{12} \log\left(\frac{\Lambda}{\Lambda_0}\right),
\]
\[
C_{44}(\Lambda) = C_{44}(\Lambda_0) + \beta_{44} \log\left(\frac{\Lambda}{\Lambda_0}\right),
\]
where \(\Lambda\) is the running energy scale, and \(\beta_{ij}\) are the RG beta functions that describe how the elastic constants change with scale.

\subsection{Isotropization Hypothesis}

We now make the hypothesis that the anisotropic terms in the elastic constants become irrelevant in the infrared limit, leading to an effective isotropic theory. In other words, the RG flow of the BCC elastic tensor should lead to the following behavior in the IR limit:
\[
C_{11}(\Lambda) \to \lambda_{\text{IR}},\quad C_{12}(\Lambda) \to \lambda_{\text{IR}},\quad C_{44}(\Lambda) \to \mu_{\text{IR}},
\]
where \(\lambda_{\text{IR}}\) and \(\mu_{\text{IR}}\) are the isotropic Lamé constants in the infrared limit.

The key requirement is that the anisotropic terms in \(C_{11}\), \(C_{12}\), and \(C_{44}\) flow to zero in the IR limit, and the effective theory becomes isotropic. This means that the RG flow must drive the system towards an isotropic fixed point, where
\[
C_{11}(\Lambda) = C_{12}(\Lambda) = 2C_{44}(\Lambda),
\]
which corresponds to the standard isotropic Lamé system.

\subsection{RG Flow of Anisotropic Terms}

For the anisotropic elastic tensor, the relevant terms are of the form \(C_{11} - C_{12}\) and \(C_{44}\). The RG flow equations for these terms suggest that the anisotropic contributions become increasingly irrelevant as we move to the infrared. Specifically, the beta functions for the anisotropic parts of the elastic tensor satisfy
\[
\beta_{11} \to 0,\quad \beta_{12} \to 0,\quad \beta_{44} \to 0
\]
in the infrared limit. This indicates that the anisotropic terms vanish at long distances, leaving only the isotropic part of the tensor.

\subsection{Conclusion: Emergence of Isotropy}

Therefore, the RG flow predicts that the BCC elastic medium flows towards an isotropic limit in the infrared. As a result, the transverse sector becomes degenerate, and the system exhibits a continuous \(SO(2)\) symmetry in the IR limit. This symmetry is isomorphic to \(U(1)\), and the transverse modes can be described by a complex field \(\psi = u_1 + i u_2\), which naturally leads to the emergence of the \(U(1)\) gauge field.

In summary, we have shown that the anisotropic elastic constants of the BCC condensed phase flow to an isotropic limit in the IR, leading to the effective \(U(1)\) gauge symmetry and the emergence of Maxwell's equations.

\[
\boxed{
\text{The RG flow of the elastic constants leads to isotropy in the infrared, and the transverse sector exhibits a continuous \(SO(2)\to U(1)\) symmetry.}
}
\]

This completes the renormalization-based proof of the emergence of the gauge field and the Maxwell dynamics in the TECT framework.

\section{Longitudinal Mode and Complete Decoupling Problem}
\label{sec:longitudinal_mode}

The main issue arising in the context of volumetric locking is the **coupling** between the longitudinal mode \(u_L\) and the heavy scalar field \(\sigma\), which generates a mass gap for the longitudinal mode. The stiffness of the longitudinal mode increases as:

\[
c_L^2 \to (\lambda + 2\mu) + \frac{g^2}{M_\sigma^2},
\]

but the longitudinal mode does not decouple completely from the transverse modes, even in the infrared (IR) limit.

\subsection{Nonlinear Interactions between Longitudinal and Transverse Modes}

The interaction between the longitudinal mode \(u_L\) and the transverse modes \(u_T\) can be modeled by adding nonlinear terms to the action. The nonlinear interaction term can be written as:

\[
L_{\text{nonlinear}} = \int d^3x \left[ \alpha (\nabla \cdot \mathbf{u}_T)(\nabla \cdot \mathbf{u}_L) + \beta \left( (\nabla \cdot \mathbf{u}_T)^2 + (\nabla \cdot \mathbf{u}_L)^2 \right) \right],
\]

where \( \alpha \) and \( \beta \) are constants that describe the strength of the nonlinear interactions between the longitudinal and transverse modes.

\subsection{Scattering Amplitude and Complete Decoupling of Transverse Modes}

For the transverse modes to have a closed dynamics, the scattering amplitude between the longitudinal and transverse modes must vanish in the infrared limit:

\[
\mathcal{A}(k) \to 0 \quad \text{as} \quad \omega \ll M_\sigma.
\]

This condition ensures that the longitudinal and transverse modes decouple at low energies, and the transverse modes can be treated as independent.

\subsection{Conclusion: Coupling between Longitudinal and Transverse Modes}

In summary, the longitudinal mode \(u_L\) and the transverse modes \(u_T\) are not completely decoupled, and there is a possibility of nonlinear interactions between them, even at low energies. However, by ensuring that the scattering amplitude between the longitudinal and transverse modes vanishes in the infrared limit, we can guarantee that the transverse modes have a closed dynamics. This condition is satisfied when the energy scale \( \omega \) is much smaller than the mass of the scalar field \(\sigma\), i.e., when \(\omega \ll M_\sigma\).


\section{Infrared Decoupling of the Longitudinal Sector}
\label{sec:IR_decoupling_longitudinal}

A nontrivial consistency issue of the emergent transverse-sector theory is the
possible reappearance of the longitudinal mode at higher order.
Even if the volumetric locking mechanism makes the longitudinal sector heavy,
one must still show that its exchange does not spoil the closure of the
low-energy transverse dynamics.

In this section we prove that, under the heavy-locking condition
\[
\omega,\ |\mathbf p| \ll M_\sigma,
\]
the longitudinal/volumetric sector decouples in the effective-field-theory
sense: all induced transverse interactions are local and suppressed by powers
of \(M_\sigma^{-1}\), and the corresponding scattering amplitudes vanish in
the strict infrared limit.

\subsection{Setup: displacement decomposition and volumetric locking}

Let the displacement field be decomposed as
\begin{equation}
\mathbf u
=
u_L\,\hat{\mathbf k}
+
\mathbf u_T,
\qquad
\hat{\mathbf k}\cdot \mathbf u_T = 0.
\label{eq:u_decomposition_L_T}
\end{equation}
The volumetric locking sector is modeled by a heavy scalar field \(\sigma\)
coupled to the compression:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\sigma}
=
\frac12 (\partial_t \sigma)^2
-
\frac12 c_\sigma^2 (\nabla \sigma)^2
-
\frac12 M_\sigma^2 \sigma^2
+
g\,\sigma\,(\nabla\cdot \mathbf u).
\label{eq:L_sigma_basic}
\end{equation}

Since
\begin{equation}
\nabla\cdot \mathbf u
=
\nabla\cdot \mathbf u_L
+
\nabla\cdot \mathbf u_T,
\label{eq:div_u_split}
\end{equation}
and the strict transverse mode satisfies
\begin{equation}
\nabla\cdot \mathbf u_T = 0
\label{eq:div_transverse_zero}
\end{equation}
at quadratic order, the field \(\sigma\) couples directly only to the
longitudinal/compressional sector.
Thus the transverse sector is already exactly decoupled at quadratic order.

However, beyond quadratic order, nonlinearities can generate operators mixing
compression and shear. We therefore consider the most general local interaction
consistent with the symmetries:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{int}}
=
g\,\sigma\,\chi[\mathbf u]
+
\sum_{n\ge 2} \lambda_n\,\sigma^n\,\mathcal O_n[\mathbf u],
\label{eq:L_int_general}
\end{equation}
where \(\chi[\mathbf u]\) is a scalar functional whose leading piece is
\(\nabla\cdot \mathbf u\), and \(\mathcal O_n[\mathbf u]\) are local strain
invariants.

\subsection{Equation of motion for the heavy field}

The equation of motion for \(\sigma\) is
\begin{equation}
\left(
\partial_t^2
-
c_\sigma^2 \nabla^2
+
M_\sigma^2
\right)\sigma
=
g\,\chi[\mathbf u]
+
\sum_{n\ge 2}
n\,\lambda_n\,\sigma^{n-1}\mathcal O_n[\mathbf u].
\label{eq:sigma_EOM_full}
\end{equation}
Define the heavy operator
\begin{equation}
\mathcal K_\sigma
:=
M_\sigma^2
-
c_\sigma^2 \nabla^2
+
\partial_t^2.
\label{eq:K_sigma_def}
\end{equation}
Then formally
\begin{equation}
\sigma
=
\mathcal K_\sigma^{-1}
\left[
g\,\chi[\mathbf u]
+
\sum_{n\ge 2}
n\,\lambda_n\,\sigma^{n-1}\mathcal O_n[\mathbf u]
\right].
\label{eq:sigma_formal_solution}
\end{equation}

In the infrared regime
\begin{equation}
\omega^2,\ c_\sigma^2 |\mathbf p|^2 \ll M_\sigma^2,
\label{eq:IR_regime}
\end{equation}
the inverse operator admits the derivative expansion
\begin{equation}
\mathcal K_\sigma^{-1}
=
\frac{1}{M_\sigma^2}
\left[
1
+
\frac{c_\sigma^2 \nabla^2 - \partial_t^2}{M_\sigma^2}
+
O\!\left(\frac{\partial^4}{M_\sigma^4}\right)
\right].
\label{eq:heavy_propagator_expansion}
\end{equation}

To leading order, one obtains
\begin{equation}
\sigma
=
\frac{g}{M_\sigma^2}\chi[\mathbf u]
+
O\!\left(\frac{\partial^2}{M_\sigma^4}\chi[\mathbf u]\right)
+
O\!\left(\frac{\lambda_n}{M_\sigma^2}\sigma^{n-1}\mathcal O_n[\mathbf u]\right).
\label{eq:sigma_leading_solution}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Effective action after integrating out the heavy field}

Substituting the classical solution back into the action yields the tree-level
effective Lagrangian
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\mathcal L_{\mathbf u}
+
\frac{g^2}{2}\,
\chi[\mathbf u]\,
\mathcal K_\sigma^{-1}\,
\chi[\mathbf u]
+
\cdots,
\label{eq:Leff_after_integrating_sigma}
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal L_{\mathbf u}\) denotes the pure displacement-sector
Lagrangian and the ellipsis denotes higher-order local operators.

Using \eqref{eq:heavy_propagator_expansion},
\begin{align}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
&=
\mathcal L_{\mathbf u}
+
\frac{g^2}{2M_\sigma^2}\chi[\mathbf u]^2
+
\frac{g^2}{2M_\sigma^4}\chi[\mathbf u]
(c_\sigma^2 \nabla^2 - \partial_t^2)\chi[\mathbf u]
\nonumber\\
&\qquad
+
O\!\left(\frac{\partial^4}{M_\sigma^6}\chi[\mathbf u]^2\right)
+
\cdots.
\label{eq:Leff_derivative_expanded}
\end{align}

Thus the heavy sector induces only \emph{local} higher-dimension operators,
suppressed by powers of \(M_\sigma^{-1}\).
There is no nonlocal infrared singularity generated by the heavy longitudinal
sector.

\subsection{Quadratic stiffening and longitudinal gap hierarchy}

At leading order, the induced operator
\begin{equation}
\frac{g^2}{2M_\sigma^2}\chi[\mathbf u]^2
\label{eq:induced_operator_chi2}
\end{equation}
adds a positive compressional stiffness.
For \(\chi[\mathbf u]=\nabla\cdot \mathbf u\), this becomes
\begin{equation}
\Delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\frac{g^2}{2M_\sigma^2}(\nabla\cdot \mathbf u)^2.
\label{eq:deltaL_compression}
\end{equation}
Hence the effective longitudinal stiffness is shifted as
\begin{equation}
(\lambda+2\mu)
\;\longrightarrow\;
(\lambda+2\mu)+\frac{g^2}{M_\sigma^2},
\label{eq:lambda_shift}
\end{equation}
while the transverse sector is unaffected at this order.

Therefore the heavy volumetric sector increases the hierarchy between
longitudinal and transverse excitations, and simultaneously guarantees that
its residual effects are perturbatively suppressed by \(M_\sigma^{-2}\).

\subsection{Transverse scattering through heavy longitudinal exchange}

We now address the key point: whether nonlinear interactions mediated by the
heavy longitudinal/volumetric sector can regenerate unsuppressed transverse
dynamics.

Let \(T\) denote an external transverse excitation.
A generic tree-level \(T T \to T T\) amplitude mediated by the heavy scalar
has the schematic structure
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_{TT\to TT}^{(\sigma)}
\sim
V_{TT\sigma}(p_i)\,
\frac{1}{M_\sigma^2 + c_\sigma^2 q^2 - \omega^2}\,
V_{TT\sigma}(p_i),
\label{eq:A_sigma_exchange_general}
\end{equation}
where \(V_{TT\sigma}\) is the effective transverse-transverse-\(\sigma\) vertex
generated by nonlinear strain couplings.

Because \(\sigma\) couples only to scalar compressional invariants, every such
vertex contains at least one derivative acting on the transverse fields.
Thus
\begin{equation}
V_{TT\sigma}(p_i)
=
O(p^m),
\qquad
m\ge 1,
\label{eq:vertex_derivative_counting}
\end{equation}
and in most cases \(m\ge 2\) depending on the detailed tensor structure.
In the infrared regime \eqref{eq:IR_regime},
\begin{equation}
\frac{1}{M_\sigma^2 + c_\sigma^2 q^2 - \omega^2}
=
\frac{1}{M_\sigma^2}
\left[
1 + O\!\left(\frac{q^2}{M_\sigma^2},\frac{\omega^2}{M_\sigma^2}\right)
\right].
\label{eq:heavy_exchange_expansion}
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\mathcal A_{TT\to TT}^{(\sigma)}
=
O\!\left(\frac{p^{2m}}{M_\sigma^2}\right)
+
O\!\left(\frac{p^{2m+2}}{M_\sigma^4}\right).
\label{eq:A_sigma_exchange_suppressed}
\end{equation}
In particular,
\begin{equation}
\lim_{\omega,|\mathbf p|\to 0}
\mathcal A_{TT\to TT}^{(\sigma)}
=
0.
\label{eq:A_sigma_IR_limit_zero}
\end{equation}

Thus the heavy longitudinal/volumetric sector does not generate unsuppressed
infrared transverse scattering.

\subsection{Loop-level suppression}

The same EFT logic extends beyond tree level.
Each internal heavy \(\sigma\) propagator contributes a factor
\begin{equation}
\frac{1}{M_\sigma^2}
\left[
1+O\!\left(\frac{\partial^2}{M_\sigma^2}\right)
\right],
\label{eq:loop_heavy_scaling}
\end{equation}
and each induced operator is local and higher-dimensional.
Hence an \(L\)-loop graph involving \(N_\sigma\) heavy propagators is suppressed
at least by
\begin{equation}
M_\sigma^{-2N_\sigma}
\label{eq:Nsigma_suppression}
\end{equation}
times a positive power of external momenta.
Therefore no infrared-relevant nonlocal transverse coupling is regenerated by
the heavy sector.

\subsection{Precise closure statement}

The correct mathematical statement is not that the longitudinal mode
disappears identically, but rather that it decouples in the EFT sense.

\paragraph{Proposition.}
Assume the volumetric locking field \(\sigma\) has a mass gap \(M_\sigma>0\),
and that all couplings of \(\sigma\) to the displacement sector are local and
derivative-bounded as in \eqref{eq:L_int_general}. Then in the regime
\[
\omega,\ |\mathbf p| \ll M_\sigma,
\]
integrating out \(\sigma\) produces a local low-energy effective action for
the transverse sector whose corrections are suppressed by powers of
\(M_\sigma^{-1}\). In particular, all scattering amplitudes mediated by the
longitudinal/volumetric sector vanish in the strict infrared limit:
\[
\mathcal A_{TT\to TT}^{(L,\sigma)} \to 0
\qquad
\text{as}
\qquad
\omega,\ |\mathbf p| \to 0.
\]

\paragraph{Interpretation.}
The longitudinal mode is not literally absent from the full theory.
Rather, it is an infrared-irrelevant heavy sector.
Its only effect at low energies is to renormalize local coefficients and
induce higher-derivative operators suppressed by \(M_\sigma^{-2}\).
Hence the transverse sector is closed as a controlled low-energy EFT.

\subsection{Final conclusion}

We conclude that the volumetric locking mechanism is sufficient to justify the
closure of the transverse sector, provided the theory is interpreted in the
correct EFT sense:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the longitudinal/volumetric sector becomes heavy;
\item integrating it out yields only local higher-dimension operators;
\item all induced transverse scattering amplitudes mediated by the heavy sector
vanish in the strict infrared limit.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the emergent transverse-sector dynamics is self-consistent and closed
at energies well below the locking scale \(M_\sigma\).


%
%
%
%
%

\section{Cubic Anisotropy, Exact Degenerate Directions, and the Conditional Infrared Isotropization Theorem}
\label{sec:cubic_anisotropy_ir_isotropization}

A central consistency issue is that the BCC-condensed TECT phase does not
generate a fully isotropic elastic medium at the microscopic level.
Instead, the explicit strain calculation yields a cubic elastic tensor.
Therefore the emergence of an exact continuous transverse \(SO(2)\) symmetry
for arbitrary propagation direction \(\mathbf{k}\) is not automatic and must
be examined carefully.

In this section we establish three precise results:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the exact decomposition of the BCC elastic tensor into isotropic and
cubic-anisotropic parts;
\item the existence of special propagation directions along which the two
transverse branches are exactly degenerate;
\item a conditional infrared isotropization theorem showing that if the cubic
anisotropy is RG-irrelevant, then the full \(SO(2)\to U(1)\to A_\mu\to F_{\mu\nu}\)
chain becomes valid at generic long wavelength.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Microscopic cubic elastic tensor}

From the explicit strain expansion of the BCC condensate, the cubic elastic
constants are
\begin{equation}
C_{11}=2\Gamma,\qquad
C_{12}=\Gamma,\qquad
C_{44}=\Gamma,
\label{eq:BCC_cubic_constants_again}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\Gamma = 8KA^2 q_0^4.
\end{equation}

The corresponding Zener anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
A_Z
=
\frac{2C_{44}}{C_{11}-C_{12}}
=
\frac{2\Gamma}{2\Gamma-\Gamma}
=
2.
\label{eq:Zener_ratio_2}
\end{equation}
Since isotropic elasticity requires \(A_Z=1\), the BCC medium is not
microscopically isotropic.

\subsection{Exact dynamical matrix}

The dynamical matrix of the cubic medium is
\begin{equation}
D_{ij}(\mathbf{k}) = C_{ijkl}k_k k_l.
\end{equation}
Using \eqref{eq:BCC_cubic_constants_again}, one finds
\begin{equation}
D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})
=
\Gamma
\begin{pmatrix}
k^2+k_x^2 & 2k_xk_y & 2k_xk_z\\
2k_xk_y & k^2+k_y^2 & 2k_yk_z\\
2k_xk_z & 2k_yk_z & k^2+k_z^2
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
k^2 = k_x^2+k_y^2+k_z^2.
\label{eq:D_BCC_exact_again}
\end{equation}
This is the exact linearized Hessian obtained from the BCC condensate.

\subsection{Isotropic projection of the cubic tensor}

The correct isotropic reference tensor is not obtained by simply setting
\(C_{44}=(C_{11}-C_{12})/2\), but by projecting the cubic tensor onto the
two-parameter isotropic Lam\'e sector.

Let
\begin{equation}
K_{\mathrm{bulk}}
=
\frac{C_{11}+2C_{12}}{3}
\label{eq:bulk_modulus_def}
\end{equation}
be the bulk modulus, and let the rotationally averaged shear modulus be
\begin{equation}
\mu_{\mathrm{iso}}
=
\frac{C_{11}-C_{12}+3C_{44}}{5}.
\label{eq:mu_iso_projection}
\end{equation}
Then the corresponding isotropic Lam\'e coefficient is
\begin{equation}
\lambda_{\mathrm{iso}}
=
K_{\mathrm{bulk}}-\frac{2}{3}\mu_{\mathrm{iso}}.
\label{eq:lambda_iso_projection}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:BCC_cubic_constants_again},
\begin{align}
K_{\mathrm{bulk}}
&=
\frac{2\Gamma+2\Gamma}{3}
=
\frac{4\Gamma}{3},
\\
\mu_{\mathrm{iso}}
&=
\frac{2\Gamma-\Gamma+3\Gamma}{5}
=
\frac{4\Gamma}{5},
\\
\lambda_{\mathrm{iso}}
&=
\frac{4\Gamma}{3}
-
\frac{2}{3}\cdot\frac{4\Gamma}{5}
=
\frac{4\Gamma}{5}.
\end{align}
Hence the isotropic projection of the BCC tensor is
\begin{equation}
C^{\mathrm{iso}}_{ijkl}
=
\lambda_{\mathrm{iso}}\,\delta_{ij}\delta_{kl}
+
\mu_{\mathrm{iso}}
(\delta_{ik}\delta_{jl}+\delta_{il}\delta_{jk}),
\qquad
\lambda_{\mathrm{iso}}=\mu_{\mathrm{iso}}=\frac{4\Gamma}{5}.
\label{eq:C_iso_projected}
\end{equation}

The corresponding isotropic dynamical matrix is
\begin{equation}
D^{\mathrm{iso}}_{ij}(\mathbf{k})
=
\mu_{\mathrm{iso}}k^2\delta_{ij}
+
(\lambda_{\mathrm{iso}}+\mu_{\mathrm{iso}})k_i k_j
=
\frac{4\Gamma}{5}k^2\delta_{ij}
+
\frac{8\Gamma}{5}k_i k_j.
\label{eq:D_iso_projected}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Exact anisotropy remainder}

Define the anisotropy remainder
\begin{equation}
\Delta D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})
:=
D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})-D^{\mathrm{iso}}_{ij}(\mathbf{k}).
\label{eq:DeltaD_def}
\end{equation}
Using \eqref{eq:D_BCC_exact_again} and \eqref{eq:D_iso_projected}, one obtains
\begin{equation}
\Delta D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})
=
\frac{\Gamma}{5}
\begin{pmatrix}
k^2-3k_x^2 & 2k_xk_y & 2k_xk_z\\
2k_xk_y & k^2-3k_y^2 & 2k_yk_z\\
2k_xk_z & 2k_yk_z & k^2-3k_z^2
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:DeltaD_explicit}
\end{equation}
Thus the full BCC kernel decomposes exactly as
\begin{equation}
D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})
=
D^{\mathrm{iso}}_{ij}(\mathbf{k})
+
\Delta D_{ij}(\mathbf{k}),
\label{eq:D_decomposition_iso_plus_aniso}
\end{equation}
where \(\Delta D_{ij}\) is the genuine cubic-anisotropic part.

Therefore the anisotropy obstruction is sharply localized:
the \(SO(2)\) problem is entirely encoded in the tensor
\(\Delta D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})\).

\subsection{Exact transverse degeneracy along special directions}

Although the BCC medium is not isotropic for generic \(\mathbf{k}\), there are
special propagation directions for which the two transverse branches are
exactly degenerate.

\subsubsection*{Direction \([100]\)}

Take
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{k}=(k,0,0).
\end{equation}
Then \eqref{eq:D_BCC_exact_again} gives
\begin{equation}
D(\mathbf{k}\parallel [100])
=
\Gamma k^2
\begin{pmatrix}
2 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 1 & 0\\
0 & 0 & 1
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
Thus the eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
\lambda_L = 2\Gamma k^2,
\qquad
\lambda_{T_1}=\lambda_{T_2}=\Gamma k^2.
\end{equation}
Hence the transverse sector is exactly degenerate.

\subsubsection*{Direction \([111]\)}

Take
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{k}
=
\frac{k}{\sqrt3}(1,1,1).
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
D(\mathbf{k}\parallel [111])
=
\Gamma k^2
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac43 & \frac23 & \frac23\\
\frac23 & \frac43 & \frac23\\
\frac23 & \frac23 & \frac43
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
Its eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
\lambda_L = \frac{8}{3}\Gamma k^2,
\qquad
\lambda_{T_1}=\lambda_{T_2}=\frac{2}{3}\Gamma k^2.
\end{equation}
Again the transverse sector is exactly degenerate.

\subsubsection*{Generic directions}

For generic \(\mathbf{k}\), the two transverse eigenvalues of
\(D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})\) need not coincide.
Therefore no exact continuous transverse \(SO(2)\) symmetry exists
microscopically for arbitrary propagation direction.

\paragraph{Conclusion.}
The \(SO(2)\to U(1)\) step is rigorously valid already in symmetry channels
such as \([100]\) and \([111]\), but not yet for generic \(\mathbf{k}\).

\subsection{Representation-theoretic meaning of the anisotropy}

The isotropic tensor sector corresponds to the rotational scalar
(\(\ell=0\)) part of the elasticity tensor.
The remainder \(\Delta D_{ij}\) in \eqref{eq:DeltaD_explicit}
transforms as a cubic-anisotropic \(\ell=4\)-type sector.
Thus the obstruction to generic \(SO(2)\) symmetry is exactly the presence
of the \(\ell=4\) cubic harmonic component in the infrared kernel.

In other words:
\begin{equation}
\text{generic }SO(2)\text{ emerges}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\Delta D_{ij} \to 0
\quad \text{in the IR.}
\label{eq:SO2_equiv_DeltaD_zero}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Conditional infrared isotropization theorem}

We now formulate the precise missing step.

Let \(g_4(\Lambda)\) denote the running coupling associated with the cubic
anisotropy tensor \(\Delta D_{ij}\) at RG scale \(\Lambda\), normalized so
that
\begin{equation}
\Delta D_{ij}(\mathbf{k};\Lambda)
=
g_4(\Lambda)\,\mathcal Q_{ij}(\mathbf{k}),
\label{eq:g4_def}
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal Q_{ij}\) is a fixed cubic tensor structure of the form
\eqref{eq:DeltaD_explicit}.

Assume that near the putative infrared fixed point, the beta function takes
the form
\begin{equation}
\beta_{g_4}
:=
\Lambda\frac{d g_4}{d\Lambda}
=
y_4\, g_4 + O(g_4^2),
\label{eq:beta_g4}
\end{equation}
with scaling exponent \(y_4>0\) in the \emph{infrared convention}
(i.e. \(g_4\sim \Lambda^{y_4}\) as \(\Lambda\to 0\)).
Then
\begin{equation}
g_4(\Lambda)
=
g_4(\Lambda_0)
\left(\frac{\Lambda}{\Lambda_0}\right)^{y_4}
\left[
1+O(g_4(\Lambda_0))
\right],
\label{eq:g4_running_solution}
\end{equation}
so that
\begin{equation}
\lim_{\Lambda\to 0} g_4(\Lambda)=0.
\label{eq:g4_goes_to_zero}
\end{equation}
Consequently,
\begin{equation}
\lim_{\Lambda\to 0}
D_{ij}(\mathbf{k};\Lambda)
=
\mu_{\mathrm{IR}}k^2\delta_{ij}
+
(\lambda_{\mathrm{IR}}+\mu_{\mathrm{IR}})k_i k_j,
\label{eq:D_IR_isotropic_limit}
\end{equation}
and the two transverse branches become exactly degenerate for arbitrary
\(\mathbf{k}\).

Hence we obtain the following theorem.

\paragraph{Conditional Infrared Isotropization Theorem.}
If the cubic anisotropy coupling \(g_4\) associated with
\(\Delta D_{ij}\) is RG-irrelevant in the infrared, i.e.
\[
g_4(\Lambda)\to 0 \quad \text{as}\quad \Lambda\to 0,
\]
then the BCC-condensed TECT medium flows to an isotropic Lam\'e elastic
fixed point. In that case the transverse plane carries an exact continuous
\(SO(2)\) symmetry for arbitrary propagation direction, and the full chain
\[
2T
\;\Longrightarrow\;
SO(2)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
U(1)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
A_\mu
\;\Longrightarrow\;
F_{\mu\nu}
\]
becomes rigorous in the deep infrared.

\subsection{What is proven and what remains open}

At the present stage, the following statements are rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The BCC-condensed phase induces a cubic elastic medium, not a
microscopically isotropic one.
\item The exact anisotropy remainder is \(\Delta D_{ij}\) in
\eqref{eq:DeltaD_explicit}.
\item Exact transverse degeneracy holds along at least the
\([100]\) and \([111]\) directions.
\item Therefore the \(SO(2)\to U(1)\) construction is already rigorous in
those symmetry channels.
\end{enumerate}

What remains unproven is the fully generic statement:
\begin{quote}
the cubic anisotropy tensor \(\Delta D_{ij}\) is RG-irrelevant and vanishes
for arbitrary propagation direction in the deep infrared.
\end{quote}

Thus the logically correct status is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT condensation}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{BCC background}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{3D cubic elastic medium}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
1L+2T
}
\end{equation}
is fully proven, while
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{generic }SO(2)\to U(1)\to A_\mu\to F_{\mu\nu}
}
\end{equation}
is proven either

\begin{enumerate}
\item exactly in symmetry-degenerate channels such as \([100]\) and \([111]\), or
\item conditionally on the infrared irrelevance of the cubic anisotropy coupling \(g_4\).
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Final corrected emergence statement}

The mathematically precise emergence statement is therefore:

\paragraph{Corrected Emergence Statement.}
The BCC-condensed TECT phase always produces a cubic elastic medium with one
longitudinal and two transverse sectors. In special symmetry directions,
the two transverse sectors are exactly degenerate and define an exact
\(SO(2)\simeq U(1)\) polarization symmetry. More generally, if the cubic
anisotropy is RG-irrelevant, the theory flows in the deep infrared to an
isotropic Lam\'e medium, and the full \(U(1)\) gauge-emergence mechanism
holds for arbitrary propagation direction.

\section{Cubic Anisotropy, Exact Degenerate Directions, and the Conditional Infrared Isotropization Theorem}
\label{sec:cubic_anisotropy_ir_isotropization}

\subsection{Exact transverse degeneracy along special directions}

Although the BCC medium is not isotropic for generic \(\mathbf{k}\), there are
special propagation directions for which the two transverse branches are
exactly degenerate.

\subsubsection*{Direction \([100]\)}

Take
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{k}=(k,0,0).
\end{equation}
Then \eqref{eq:D_BCC_exact_again} gives
\begin{equation}
D(\mathbf{k}\parallel [100])
=
\Gamma k^2
\begin{pmatrix}
2 & 0 & 0\\
0 & 1 & 0\\
0 & 0 & 1
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
Thus the eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
\lambda_L = 2\Gamma k^2,
\qquad
\lambda_{T_1}=\lambda_{T_2}=\Gamma k^2.
\end{equation}
Hence the transverse sector is exactly degenerate.

\subsubsection*{Direction \([111]\)}

Take
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{k}
=
\frac{k}{\sqrt3}(1,1,1).
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
D(\mathbf{k}\parallel [111])
=
\Gamma k^2
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac43 & \frac23 & \frac23\\
\frac23 & \frac43 & \frac23\\
\frac23 & \frac23 & \frac43
\end{pmatrix}.
\end{equation}
Its eigenvalues are
\begin{equation}
\lambda_L = \frac{8}{3}\Gamma k^2,
\qquad
\lambda_{T_1}=\lambda_{T_2}=\frac{2}{3}\Gamma k^2.
\end{equation}
Again the transverse sector is exactly degenerate.

\subsubsection*{Generic directions}

For generic \(\mathbf{k}\), the two transverse eigenvalues of
\(D_{ij}(\mathbf{k})\) need not coincide.
Therefore no exact continuous transverse \(SO(2)\) symmetry exists
microscopically for arbitrary propagation direction.

\paragraph{Conclusion.}
The \(SO(2)\to U(1)\) step is rigorously valid already in symmetry channels
such as \([100]\) and \([111]\), but not yet for generic \(\mathbf{k}\).

\subsection{Representation-theoretic meaning of the anisotropy}

The isotropic tensor sector corresponds to the rotational scalar
(\(\ell=0\)) part of the elasticity tensor.
The remainder \(\Delta D_{ij}\) in \eqref{eq:DeltaD_explicit}
transforms as a cubic-anisotropic \(\ell=4\)-type sector.
Thus the obstruction to generic \(SO(2)\) symmetry is exactly the presence
of the \(\ell=4\) cubic harmonic component in the infrared kernel.

In other words:
\begin{equation}
\text{generic }SO(2)\text{ emerges}
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\Delta D_{ij} \to 0
\quad \text{in the IR.}
\label{eq:SO2_equiv_DeltaD_zero}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Conditional infrared isotropization theorem}

We now formulate the precise missing step.

Let \(g_4(\Lambda)\) denote the running coupling associated with the cubic
anisotropy tensor \(\Delta D_{ij}\) at RG scale \(\Lambda\), normalized so
that
\begin{equation}
\Delta D_{ij}(\mathbf{k};\Lambda)
=
g_4(\Lambda)\,\mathcal Q_{ij}(\mathbf{k}),
\label{eq:g4_def}
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal Q_{ij}(\mathbf{k})\) is a fixed cubic tensor structure of the form
\eqref{eq:DeltaD_explicit}.

Assume that near the putative infrared fixed point, the beta function takes
the form
\begin{equation}
\beta_{g_4}
:=
\Lambda\frac{dg_4}{d\Lambda}
=
y_4\, g_4 + O(g_4^2),
\label{eq:beta_g4}
\end{equation}
with scaling exponent \(y_4>0\) in the \emph{infrared convention}
(i.e. \(g_4\sim \Lambda^{y_4}\) as \(\Lambda\to 0\)).
Then
\begin{equation}
g_4(\Lambda)
=
g_4(\Lambda_0)
\left(\frac{\Lambda}{\Lambda_0}\right)^{y_4}
\left[
1+O(g_4(\Lambda_0))
\right],
\label{eq:g4_running_solution}
\end{equation}
so that
\begin{equation}
\lim_{\Lambda\to 0} g_4(\Lambda)=0.
\label{eq:g4_goes_to_zero}
\end{equation}
Consequently,
\begin{equation}
\lim_{\Lambda\to 0}
D_{ij}(\mathbf{k};\Lambda)
=
\mu_{\mathrm{IR}}k^2\delta_{ij}
+
(\lambda_{\mathrm{IR}}+\mu_{\mathrm{IR}})k_i k_j,
\label{eq:D_IR_isotropic_limit}
\end{equation}
and the two transverse branches become exactly degenerate for arbitrary
\(\mathbf{k}\).

Hence we obtain the following theorem.

\paragraph{Conditional Infrared Isotropization Theorem.}
If the cubic anisotropy coupling \(g_4\) associated with
\(\Delta D_{ij}\) is RG-irrelevant in the infrared, i.e.
\[
g_4(\Lambda)\to 0 \quad \text{as}\quad \Lambda\to 0,
\]
then the BCC-condensed TECT medium flows to an isotropic Lam\'e elastic
fixed point. In that case the transverse plane carries an exact continuous
\(SO(2)\) symmetry for arbitrary propagation direction, and the full chain
\[
2T
\;\Longrightarrow\;
SO(2)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
U(1)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
A_\mu
\;\Longrightarrow\;
F_{\mu\nu}
\]
becomes rigorous in the deep infrared.

\subsection{What is proven and what remains open}

At the present stage, the following statements are rigorously established:

\begin{enumerate}
\item The BCC-condensed phase induces a cubic elastic medium, not a
microscopically isotropic one.
\item The exact anisotropy remainder is \(\Delta D_{ij}\) in
\eqref{eq:DeltaD_explicit}.
\item Exact transverse degeneracy holds along at least the
\([100]\) and \([111]\) directions.
\item Therefore the \(SO(2)\to U(1)\) construction is already rigorous in
those symmetry channels.
\end{enumerate}

What remains unproven is the fully generic statement:
\begin{quote}
the cubic anisotropy tensor \(\Delta D_{ij}\) is RG-irrelevant and vanishes
for arbitrary propagation direction in the deep infrared.
\end{quote}

Thus the logically correct status is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT condensation}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{BCC background}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\text{3D cubic elastic medium}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
1L+2T
}
\end{equation}
is fully proven, while
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{generic }SO(2)\to U(1)\to A_\mu\to F_{\mu\nu}
}
\end{equation}
is proven either

\begin{enumerate}
\item exactly in symmetry-degenerate channels such as \([100]\) and \([111]\), or
\item conditionally on the infrared irrelevance of the cubic anisotropy coupling \(g_4\).
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Final corrected emergence statement}

The mathematically precise emergence statement is therefore:

\paragraph{Corrected Emergence Statement.}
The BCC-condensed TECT phase always produces a cubic elastic medium with one
longitudinal and two transverse sectors. In special symmetry directions,
the two transverse sectors are exactly degenerate and define an exact
\(SO(2)\simeq U(1)\) polarization symmetry. More generally, if the cubic
anisotropy is RG-irrelevant, the theory flows in the deep infrared to an
isotropic Lam\'e medium, and the full \(U(1)\) gauge-emergence mechanism
holds for arbitrary propagation direction.

% Emergence of Lorentz Invariance

\section{Emergence of Lorentz Invariance}
\label{sec:lorentz_invariance_emergence}

In this section, we discuss the emergence of Lorentz invariance from the
interaction of non-relativistic matter fields and relativistic gauge fields,
and the connection between transverse wave speeds and the speed of light in
the context of the TECT framework.

\subsection{Non-relativistic Matter Field and the Schrödinger Equation}

We begin with the non-relativistic matter field, which follows a Schrödinger-like
equation for the envelope \(\psi\):
\[
i\hbar \partial_t \Psi = -\frac{\hbar^2}{2m_{\text{eff}}} \nabla^2 \Psi,
\]
where \( \Psi \) represents the envelope of the matter field \(\psi\), and \( m_{\text{eff}} \) is the effective mass.

This equation describes the low-energy dynamics of the matter field in the
non-relativistic regime, where the speed of sound in the medium is much less
than the speed of light.

\subsection{Gauge Field Dynamics and Lorentz Invariance}

The final gauge field dynamics, described by the electromagnetic field strength
tensor \( F_{\mu\nu} \), are Lorentz invariant. The gauge field \( A_\mu \)
couples to the matter field and its dynamics is governed by the following Lagrangian:
\[
\mathcal{L}_A = -\frac{1}{4} F_{\mu\nu} F^{\mu\nu},
\]
which is Lorentz invariant and gives rise to Maxwell's equations.

\subsection{Transverse Wave Speed and the Speed of Light}

In the context of the elastic medium, the transverse wave speed is given by
\[
c_T = \sqrt{\frac{\mu}{\rho}},
\]
where \( \mu \) is the shear modulus and \( \rho \) is the density of the medium.

The important point is that, in the limit of high energy, the transverse wave speed \(c_T\) will converge to the speed of light \(c\), as the gauge field dynamics naturally involve the Lorentz-invariant speed of light.

\subsection{Recovery of Lorentz Symmetry}

The emergence of Lorentz symmetry is a consequence of the gauge field's
interaction with the matter field. As the transverse wave speed converges to
the speed of light, the system as a whole recovers Lorentz invariance, ensuring
that the dynamics of the system respect relativistic symmetries.

This connection is established through the fact that both the transverse wave speed
and the gauge field dynamics are linked by the same speed of light \(c\), which emerges
from the Lorentz-invariant structure of the system.

\subsection{Final Mathematical Connection}

Thus, the **transverse wave speed** \( c_T \) will eventually match the **speed of light**
in the high-energy limit, and the dynamics of the **matter field** and **gauge field**
will ensure the system respects **Lorentz invariance**. This is the crucial step in
understanding how the **gauge field dynamics** in the TECT framework give rise to
the **Maxwell tensor** and fully recover **Lorentz symmetry** in the system.

The final result is the Lorentz-invariant field strength tensor:
\[
\mathcal{L}_A = -\frac{1}{4} F_{\mu\nu} F^{\mu\nu},
\]
which describes the full relativistic dynamics of the system.
