\section{Mathematical Vulnerabilities and Required Strengthening Program}
\label{sec:math_vulnerabilities_program}

At the present stage, the TECT \(\to\) BCC \(\to\) elastic \(\to\) transverse
sector \(\to\) \(U(1)\) emergence chain is structurally compelling, but several
mathematical points still require explicit closure before the theory can be
regarded as fully controlled.

The purpose of this section is to isolate the main vulnerabilities and convert
them into a concrete strengthening program. The issues are ordered by priority.

\subsection{Priority I: Rigorous out-integration of heavy modes}
\label{subsec:priority1_outintegration}

The first major issue is the rigor of integrating out the heavy longitudinal
and volumetric sector. The present argument establishes EFT decoupling at the
level of a derivative expansion, but the following points remain to be shown
explicitly:

\begin{enumerate}
\item control of nonlocal terms in the effective action,
\item matching across the heavy-light boundary layer,
\item finite-\(k\) corrections beyond the strict \(\omega,|\mathbf p|\ll M_\sigma\) limit.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Required theorem.}
Let \(X_H\) denote the heavy sector and \(X_L\) the light transverse sector.
The effective action
\begin{equation}
e^{i\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}[X_L]}
=
\int \mathcal D X_H\, e^{iS[X_L,X_H]}
\end{equation}
must be shown to admit the expansion
\begin{equation}
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}[X_L]
=
\int d^4x\,
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{local}}[X_L]
+
\Gamma_{\mathrm{nonlocal}}[X_L],
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\Gamma_{\mathrm{nonlocal}}[X_L]
=
O\!\left(\frac{\partial^2}{M_H^2}\right)
\end{equation}
in the infrared regime.

\paragraph{Required calculation.}
A systematic matching computation should be performed at finite external
momentum:
\begin{equation}
\Gamma^{(n)}_{\mathrm{full}}(p_i)
-
\Gamma^{(n)}_{\mathrm{EFT}}(p_i)
=
O\!\left(\frac{p^{m+1}}{M_H^{m+1}}\right)
\end{equation}
for the relevant \(n\)-point functions. In practice, the key objects are the
two-point and four-point transverse amplitudes.

\paragraph{Deliverable.}
A dedicated appendix should contain:
\begin{enumerate}
\item explicit heavy-field propagator expansion at finite \(k\),
\item matching of the quadratic kernel,
\item matching of the quartic transverse scattering amplitude,
\item proof that nonlocal kernels are suppressed by \(M_H^{-2}\).
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Priority II: Nonlinear and lattice corrections}
\label{subsec:priority2_lattice_nonlinear}

The second major issue is the effect of nonlinear terms and lattice corrections,
especially the \(O(a^2k^4)\) terms inherited from the microscopic BCC medium.

At the microscopic level the elastic kernel is not exactly isotropic and takes
the schematic form
\begin{equation}
D_{ij}(\mathbf k)
=
D^{\mathrm{iso}}_{ij}(\mathbf k)
+
\Delta D^{\mathrm{cub}}_{ij}(\mathbf k)
+
a^2 \mathcal R_{ij}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)
+
O(a^4k^6).
\end{equation}
These corrections may in principle:
\begin{enumerate}
\item split the transverse degeneracy,
\item explicitly break the emergent \(U(1)\),
\item generate a gauge-field mass term at loop level,
\item induce higher-derivative symmetry-breaking operators.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Required one-loop test.}
Let the emergent connection be \(A_\mu\). One must compute whether the
one-loop effective action generates
\begin{equation}
\delta \Gamma[A]
\supset
\frac12 m_A^2 A_\mu A^\mu
\end{equation}
or not. Gauge emergence remains viable only if
\begin{equation}
m_A^2 = 0
\end{equation}
to the order under consideration, or if any nonzero contribution is shown to
be an artifact of working away from the true isotropic infrared fixed point.

\paragraph{Required operator classification.}
The full EFT should be organized as
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\mathcal L_{U(1)}
+
\sum_n \frac{c_n}{\Lambda_*^{d_n-4}}\mathcal O_n,
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal O_n\) are all symmetry-allowed operators, including lattice
anisotropy operators. One must determine which of these:
\begin{enumerate}
\item preserve the emergent \(U(1)\),
\item softly break it,
\item are RG-irrelevant,
\item can radiatively induce a mass.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Deliverable.}
A one-loop computation of the polarization tensor and the anisotropy-induced
self-energy should be provided, together with a table of higher-derivative
operators of order \(a^2k^4\).

\subsection{Priority III: Global phase bundle and topological obstructions}
\label{subsec:priority3_bundle}

The local spin-connection picture is clear, but the global structure of the
transverse polarization bundle has not yet been analyzed.

Locally, the emergent \(U(1)\) field arises from frame redundancy on the
two-dimensional transverse polarization plane. Globally, however, one must
consider whether the local trivializations patch together consistently over the
relevant momentum-space or spacetime manifold.

\paragraph{Required geometric structure.}
Let \(\mathcal U_\alpha\) be local patches and let
\begin{equation}
\psi_\alpha = e^{i\chi_{\alpha\beta}}\psi_\beta
\qquad
\text{on }
\mathcal U_\alpha\cap \mathcal U_\beta.
\end{equation}
Then the transition functions \(e^{i\chi_{\alpha\beta}}\) define a
\(U(1)\)-bundle, and consistency requires the cocycle condition
\begin{equation}
\chi_{\alpha\beta}
+
\chi_{\beta\gamma}
+
\chi_{\gamma\alpha}
\in 2\pi \mathbb Z.
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Required topological check.}
One must determine whether the emergent connection has nontrivial first Chern
class
\begin{equation}
c_1
=
\frac{1}{2\pi}\,[F]
\in H^2(M,\mathbb Z),
\end{equation}
for the relevant base manifold \(M\). This matters because defects, holes,
or singular polarization textures may obstruct a globally trivial bundle.

\paragraph{Physical meaning.}
If \(c_1\neq 0\), the emergent \(U(1)\) sector may support quantized flux,
topological defects, or monopole-like singular structures. If \(c_1=0\), the
bundle is globally trivial and the local complex-field picture extends
globally without obstruction.

\paragraph{Deliverable.}
A separate geometric appendix should:
\begin{enumerate}
\item define the base manifold of the polarization bundle,
\item write explicit transition functions,
\item determine whether \(c_1\) can be nonzero,
\item classify defects and singularities compatible with the TECT background.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Priority IV: Normalization, quantization, and anomaly control}
\label{subsec:priority4_quantization}

The next issue is the normalization and quantization of the emergent gauge
sector. In particular, the effective coefficient \(\kappa\) in
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_A
=
-\frac{\kappa}{4}F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu}
\end{equation}
has so far been introduced on EFT grounds, but not yet derived from the
microscopic TECT parameters.

\paragraph{Required derivation of \(\kappa\).}
One needs an explicit matching relation of the form
\begin{equation}
\kappa
=
\kappa\!\left(K,\rho,A,q_0,M_\sigma,\dots\right),
\end{equation}
obtained by integrating out fast elastic and/or volumetric fluctuations.

\paragraph{Ward identities.}
If the \(U(1)\) structure is truly emergent, then the low-energy theory must
satisfy a Ward identity of the form
\begin{equation}
p_\mu \Pi^{\mu\nu}(p)=0
\end{equation}
for the gauge-field self-energy \(\Pi^{\mu\nu}\). This is the operational test
that the emergent symmetry is not spoiled by quantization at the order being
considered.

\paragraph{Anomaly issue.}
For a bosonic \(U(1)\) theory in \(3+1\) dimensions there is no perturbative
gauge anomaly in the ordinary sense, but one must still verify that no
effective symmetry-breaking Jacobian or regularization artifact is generated
in the emergent construction. In particular, the quantization procedure must
be compatible with the local frame redundancy interpretation.

\paragraph{Deliverable.}
The quantization package should contain:
\begin{enumerate}
\item derivation of \(\kappa\) from microscopic parameters,
\item explicit one-loop polarization tensor,
\item proof of transversality,
\item demonstration that the emergent \(U(1)\) symmetry survives quantization
to the order analyzed.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Priority V: Physical parameter estimates}
\label{subsec:priority5_parameter_estimates}

The final major strengthening step is numerical and phenomenological.
Even a structurally elegant theory remains incomplete unless the characteristic
scales are estimated.

The parameters that must be estimated are:
\begin{equation}
m_{\mathrm{long}},
\qquad
m_{\mathrm{trans}},
\qquad
q,
\qquad
\kappa.
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Minimal scaling relations.}
The current framework already suggests relations of the form
\begin{equation}
m_{\mathrm{eff}}
\sim
\frac{\hbar\omega_*}{c_T^2},
\qquad
c_T^2=\frac{\mu}{\rho},
\qquad
m_{\mathrm{long}}^2 \sim M_\sigma^2 + O(g^2),
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\kappa
\sim
\frac{\text{polarization stiffness}}{\text{locking scale}^{\,\alpha}}
\end{equation}
for some model-dependent exponent \(\alpha\).
These scaling laws should be turned into explicit parameter windows.

\paragraph{Numerical program.}
A credible phenomenological section should estimate:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the scale separation \(m_{\mathrm{long}}/m_{\mathrm{trans}}\),
\item the expected magnitude of anisotropy corrections,
\item the effective wave speed \(c_T\),
\item the normalization of \(q\) and \(\kappa\),
\item the energy range where the transverse \(U(1)\) EFT is valid.
\end{enumerate}

\paragraph{Deliverable.}
At minimum, the paper should provide parametric bounds. Ideally, it should
also include at least one benchmark numerical example showing that the EFT
window is nonempty:
\begin{equation}
m_{\mathrm{trans}}
\ll
E
\ll
m_{\mathrm{long}},
\qquad
a|k| \ll 1,
\qquad
|g_4(k)| \ll 1.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Priority-ordered roadmap}

We summarize the strengthening program as follows:

\paragraph{Stage I.}
Rigorous heavy-mode out-integration and finite-\(k\) matching.

\paragraph{Stage II.}
One-loop control of lattice and nonlinear corrections, including the gauge-field
mass test.

\paragraph{Stage III.}
Global bundle analysis and topological consistency of the emergent \(U(1)\).

\paragraph{Stage IV.}
Microscopic derivation and quantization of the gauge sector, including
\(\kappa\), Ward identities, and anomaly control.

\paragraph{Stage V.}
Numerical scale estimates and phenomenological parameter windows.

\subsection{Final assessment}

The theory is strongest where it already provides:
\begin{enumerate}
\item a concrete BCC condensate,
\item an explicit cubic elastic tensor,
\item a controlled infrared transverse-sector EFT,
\item a geometric mechanism for local \(U(1)\) emergence.
\end{enumerate}

The main unfinished work is not conceptual but mathematical:
the derivation must now be upgraded from a structurally compelling EFT picture
to a quantitatively controlled and globally consistent effective theory.

In short, the remaining task is to prove that the emergent gauge sector is not
only geometrically natural, but also:
\begin{equation}
\text{local, stable, globally consistent, quantizable, and parametrically valid.}
\end{equation}

%
% Priority I: out-integration 엄밀성
%

\section{Rigorous Out-Integration of the Heavy Longitudinal Sector}
\label{sec:heavy_mode_outintegration}

The emergence of the transverse effective field theory requires that
the heavy longitudinal sector can be integrated out in a controlled way.
In particular we must demonstrate that the resulting effective action
is local in the infrared and that all nonlocal corrections are suppressed
by powers of the heavy mass scale.

\subsection{Heavy–light field decomposition}

Let the full field content be decomposed as

\begin{equation}
X = (X_L , X_H),
\end{equation}

where

\begin{itemize}
\item \(X_L\) denotes the light transverse sector,
\item \(X_H\) denotes the heavy longitudinal / volumetric sector.
\end{itemize}

The microscopic action takes the form

\begin{equation}
S[X_L , X_H]
=
S_L[X_L]
+
S_H[X_H]
+
S_{\mathrm{int}}[X_L , X_H].
\end{equation}

The heavy sector is assumed to have a mass gap

\begin{equation}
M_H \sim m_{\mathrm{long}}.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Definition of the effective action}

The low-energy effective action is defined by functional integration
over the heavy sector:

\begin{equation}
e^{i\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}[X_L]}
=
\int \mathcal D X_H
\exp
\left(
iS[X_L , X_H]
\right).
\end{equation}

To evaluate this integral we expand around the classical solution
\(X_H^\star\) defined by

\begin{equation}
\frac{\delta S}{\delta X_H}\Big|_{X_H^\star}=0 .
\end{equation}

Let

\begin{equation}
X_H = X_H^\star + \eta .
\end{equation}

Expanding the action gives

\begin{equation}
S
=
S[X_L , X_H^\star]
+
\frac12
\eta \, \mathcal{K}_H \, \eta
+
O(\eta^3)
\end{equation}

where

\begin{equation}
\mathcal K_H
=
\frac{\delta^2 S}{\delta X_H^2}
\Big|_{X_H^\star}.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Gaussian integration}

To leading order the functional integral becomes Gaussian:

\begin{equation}
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}[X_L]
=
S[X_L , X_H^\star]
+
\frac{i}{2}\log \det \mathcal K_H
+
O(M_H^{-4}).
\end{equation}

Thus the effective action separates into

\begin{equation}
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\Gamma_{\mathrm{tree}}
+
\Gamma_{\mathrm{loop}} .
\end{equation}

\subsection{Momentum expansion of the heavy propagator}

The heavy propagator has the structure

\begin{equation}
G_H(\omega ,\mathbf p)
=
\frac{1}{\omega^2 - c_H^2 p^2 - M_H^2}.
\end{equation}

For infrared momenta

\begin{equation}
\omega , |\mathbf p| \ll M_H
\end{equation}

we expand

\begin{equation}
G_H
=
-\frac{1}{M_H^2}
\left[
1
+
\frac{\omega^2 - c_H^2 p^2}{M_H^2}
+
O\!\left(\frac{p^4}{M_H^4}\right)
\right].
\end{equation}

This expansion demonstrates that heavy exchange produces
local operators in the effective theory.

\subsection{Structure of the effective action}

Substituting the expansion of the propagator yields

\begin{equation}
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}[X_L]
=
\int d^4x
\,
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}[X_L]
+
\Gamma_{\mathrm{nonlocal}}[X_L]
\end{equation}

with

\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\mathcal L_0
+
\frac{1}{M_H^2}\mathcal O_2
+
\frac{1}{M_H^4}\mathcal O_4
+\dots
\end{equation}

where \(\mathcal O_n\) contains operators with \(n\) derivatives.

The nonlocal remainder satisfies

\begin{equation}
\Gamma_{\mathrm{nonlocal}}
=
O\!\left(
\frac{\partial^2}{M_H^2}
\right).
\end{equation}

\subsection{Finite momentum matching}

To verify the EFT we compare the full theory and EFT
two-point functions.

Let

\begin{equation}
\Gamma^{(2)}_{\mathrm{full}}(p)
\end{equation}

be the exact transverse kernel and

\begin{equation}
\Gamma^{(2)}_{\mathrm{EFT}}(p)
\end{equation}

the EFT prediction.

Matching requires

\begin{equation}
\Gamma^{(2)}_{\mathrm{full}}(p)
-
\Gamma^{(2)}_{\mathrm{EFT}}(p)
=
O\!\left(
\frac{p^4}{M_H^2}
\right).
\end{equation}

Thus the effective theory correctly reproduces
all infrared amplitudes up to controlled corrections.

\subsection{Decoupling theorem}

We therefore obtain the following result.

\paragraph{Heavy-mode decoupling theorem.}

If the longitudinal sector possesses a gap \(M_H\),
then integrating out the heavy fields produces
a local infrared effective action for the transverse sector:

\begin{equation}
\Gamma_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\int d^4x
\,
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
+
O\!\left(
\frac{\partial^2}{M_H^2}
\right).
\end{equation}

Consequently the transverse dynamics form a
self-consistent effective field theory in the regime

\begin{equation}
\omega , |k| \ll M_H .
\end{equation}

%
% 1-loop gauge mass test 눈문핵심개산
%

\section{One-Loop Gauge-Mass Test and the Fate of the Emergent \(U(1)\) Symmetry}
\label{sec:oneloop_gauge_mass_test}

A central consistency test of the emergent \(U(1)\) sector is whether the
gauge connection \(A_\mu\) remains massless after quantum corrections.
At the level of the low-energy effective theory, this reduces to the
one-loop structure of the gauge-field self-energy
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p).
\]
The question is whether the effective action generates a Proca term
\[
\frac12 m_A^2 A_\mu A^\mu
\]
at one loop.

The answer depends sharply on whether the emergent local \(U(1)\) redundancy
is exact or only approximate.

\subsection{Gauge-invariant reference EFT}

Consider the gauge-covariant low-energy action for the complex transverse field
\(\psi\):
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{EFT}}
=
(D_\mu\psi)^*(D^\mu\psi)
-
V(|\psi|)
-
\frac{\kappa}{4}F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu},
\qquad
D_\mu=\partial_\mu-iqA_\mu.
\label{eq:gauge_invariant_EFT}
\end{equation}
The quadratic part of the matter sector is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\psi}^{(2)}
=
\partial_\mu\psi^*\partial^\mu\psi
-
m_\psi^2 |\psi|^2
-
iqA_\mu\left(\psi^*\partial^\mu\psi-\psi\partial^\mu\psi^*\right)
+
q^2A_\mu A^\mu |\psi|^2.
\label{eq:matter_quadratic_couplings}
\end{equation}

The cubic and quartic vertices relevant for the one-loop polarization tensor are:

\begin{itemize}
\item the three-point vertex
\begin{equation}
V^{(3)}_\mu(k+p,k)
=
iq\,(2k+p)_\mu,
\label{eq:three_point_vertex}
\end{equation}
\item the seagull vertex
\begin{equation}
V^{(4)}_{\mu\nu}
=
2iq^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}
\end{equation}
up to convention-dependent factors.
\end{itemize}

\subsection{One-loop polarization tensor}

At one loop, the gauge-field self-energy receives two contributions:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the bubble graph with two three-point vertices,
\item the seagull graph with one four-point vertex.
\end{enumerate}

Using a translationally invariant regulator, the total polarization tensor is
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)
=
q^2
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
\frac{(2k+p)_\mu(2k+p)_\nu}
{\bigl(k^2-m_\psi^2+i0\bigr)\bigl((k+p)^2-m_\psi^2+i0\bigr)}
-
\frac{2\eta_{\mu\nu}}{k^2-m_\psi^2+i0}
\right].
\label{eq:Pi_munu_full}
\end{equation}

The second term is the seagull subtraction required by gauge invariance.

\subsection{Ward identity and transversality}

If the local \(U(1)\) redundancy is exact, the effective action satisfies the
Ward identity
\begin{equation}
p_\mu \Pi^{\mu\nu}(p)=0.
\label{eq:Ward_transversality}
\end{equation}
Consequently, \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\) must have the transverse form
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)
=
\left(
p_\mu p_\nu - p^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}
\right)\Pi(p^2).
\label{eq:Pi_transverse_form}
\end{equation}
A Proca mass term would instead contribute
\begin{equation}
\Pi^{\mathrm{mass}}_{\mu\nu}(p)=m_A^2\eta_{\mu\nu},
\end{equation}
which is not transverse, since
\begin{equation}
p_\mu \Pi^{\mathrm{mass}\,\mu\nu}(p)=m_A^2 p^\nu \neq 0.
\end{equation}
Therefore exact transversality forbids a gauge-boson mass:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
m_A^2 = 0
\qquad
\text{if the emergent local \(U(1)\) symmetry is exact.}
}
\label{eq:massless_if_exact_U1}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Explicit zero-momentum test}

A direct one-loop mass test is obtained by evaluating \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\).
From \eqref{eq:Pi_munu_full},
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
=
q^2
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
\frac{4k_\mu k_\nu}{(k^2-m_\psi^2+i0)^2}
-
\frac{2\eta_{\mu\nu}}{k^2-m_\psi^2+i0}
\right].
\label{eq:Pi_zero_pre}
\end{equation}
Using rotational/Lorentz symmetry of the loop integral,
\begin{equation}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}\,
\frac{k_\mu k_\nu}{(k^2-m_\psi^2+i0)^2}
=
\frac{\eta_{\mu\nu}}{d}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}\,
\frac{k^2}{(k^2-m_\psi^2+i0)^2},
\label{eq:rotational_reduction}
\end{equation}
so
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
=
q^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}
\left[
\frac{4}{d}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}\,
\frac{k^2}{(k^2-m_\psi^2+i0)^2}
-
2
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}\,
\frac{1}{k^2-m_\psi^2+i0}
\right].
\label{eq:Pi_zero_mid}
\end{equation}
In any gauge-invariant regularization scheme, the bubble and seagull pieces
cancel exactly so that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0.
}
\label{eq:Pi_zero_zero}
\end{equation}
Thus no mass term is generated at one loop.

\subsection{Meaning of the one-loop result}

Equation \eqref{eq:Pi_zero_zero} is not merely a technical cancellation.
It expresses the fact that the local polarization-frame redundancy acts as a
true gauge symmetry in the exact infrared theory. As long as that redundancy
is preserved by regularization and by the effective operators retained,
the emergent gauge field remains massless to one loop.

\subsection{Anisotropy and symmetry-breaking insertions}

The crucial caveat is that the microscopic BCC background is not exactly
isotropic. Hence the EFT may contain symmetry-breaking operators induced by
lattice corrections. Write the effective action schematically as
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\mathcal L_{U(1)}
+
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{aniso}},
\label{eq:Leff_split_U1_aniso}
\end{equation}
where \(\mathcal L_{U(1)}\) is the gauge-invariant sector and
\(\delta\mathcal L_{\mathrm{aniso}}\) contains lattice-induced corrections.

At lowest order one expects terms of the form
\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{aniso}}
=
a^2\, c_1\, \mathcal O_{(4)}
+
a^2\, c_2\, \mathcal O_{(2,2)}
+\cdots,
\label{eq:aniso_ops_general}
\end{equation}
where the operators \(\mathcal O_{(4)}\), \(\mathcal O_{(2,2)}\) carry four
derivatives and transform only under the cubic point group, not under full
rotational symmetry.

There are then two logically distinct possibilities:

\paragraph{Case A: anisotropy preserves the local \(U(1)\) redundancy.}
In this case the correction modifies the kinetic sector but still satisfies
the Ward identity. Then
\begin{equation}
p_\mu \Pi^{\mu\nu}(p)=0
\end{equation}
continues to hold, and no gauge mass is generated:
\begin{equation}
m_A^2=0.
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Case B: anisotropy breaks the emergent local \(U(1)\) redundancy.}
In this case the one-loop polarization tensor need not be transverse.
Then a nonzero zero-momentum limit is allowed:
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
=
m_A^2 \eta_{\mu\nu}
+
O(a^2\Lambda^4),
\label{eq:Pi_zero_mass_generated}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
m_A^2
\sim
a^2 c_i\, \Lambda_{\mathrm{UV}}^{4-d}
\end{equation}
up to model-dependent coefficients.

Therefore the one-loop mass test reduces to checking whether the anisotropy
operators preserve or violate the local polarization-bundle redundancy.

\subsection{Correct criterion for gauge survival}

The physically correct criterion is not simply isotropy, but
\emph{exact transversality} of the quantum effective action. Define the
renormalized polarization tensor by
\begin{equation}
\Pi^{R}_{\mu\nu}(p)
=
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)
-
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
-
(\text{wavefunction counterterms}).
\end{equation}
Then:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The emergent gauge field remains massless}
\iff
p_\mu \Pi^{R\,\mu\nu}(p)=0
\iff
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0.
}
\label{eq:masslessness_criterion}
\end{equation}

\subsection{One-loop gauge-mass theorem}

We may now formulate the result precisely.

\paragraph{Theorem (one-loop gauge-mass test).}
Consider the low-energy transverse EFT derived from the BCC-condensed TECT phase.

\begin{enumerate}
\item If the effective action admits an exact local \(U(1)\) redundancy,
then the one-loop polarization tensor is transverse:
\[
p_\mu \Pi^{\mu\nu}(p)=0,
\]
and therefore the emergent gauge field remains massless:
\[
m_A^2=0.
\]

\item If lattice-induced anisotropy operators explicitly break the local
\(U(1)\) redundancy, then transversality may fail and a nonzero one-loop
mass
\[
m_A^2 = \Pi_T(0)
\]
may be generated.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the one-loop mass test is the sharp diagnostic separating a genuinely
emergent gauge symmetry from a merely approximate phase redundancy.

\subsection{Practical consequence}

The next required computation is therefore explicit:
one must classify the leading \(O(a^2k^4)\) lattice operators and evaluate
their contribution to the one-loop polarization tensor. In practice, the task is

\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{aniso}}
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\delta \Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\delta \Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
\end{equation}

and then determine whether
\begin{equation}
\delta \Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
\delta \Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\neq 0.
\end{equation}

Only after this test can one claim that the Maxwell sector survives quantum
corrections in the full lattice-corrected theory.

\subsection{Final conclusion}

The one-loop analysis shows the following.

\begin{itemize}
\item In the exact gauge-invariant infrared EFT, the emergent connection is
massless at one loop.
\item The only possible obstruction is explicit symmetry breaking from lattice
anisotropy or higher-derivative corrections.
\item Therefore the fate of the emergent Maxwell sector is controlled by a
single concrete computation: the zero-momentum polarization tensor in the
presence of the leading \(O(a^2k^4)\) anisotropy operators.
\end{itemize}

This makes the one-loop mass test the decisive next step in upgrading the
TECT gauge-emergence scenario from a geometrically natural EFT picture to a
fully controlled quantum effective theory.

\section{Classification of Leading \(O(a^2k^4)\) Anisotropy Operators}
\label{sec:anisotropy_operator_classification}

The decisive next step in the one-loop gauge-mass test is the classification
of the leading lattice-induced operators of order \(O(a^2 k^4)\).
These operators arise because the microscopic BCC background is only cubic,
not fully rotationally invariant. The question is which of them preserve the
emergent local \(U(1)\) redundancy of the transverse polarization bundle, and
which of them break it.

The answer determines whether the one-loop polarization tensor remains
transverse and whether a gauge mass can be generated.

\subsection{General organizing principle}

Let \(\psi\) denote the complex transverse field and \(A_\mu\) the emergent
\(U(1)\) connection. The effective action is organized as
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
=
\mathcal L_{U(1)}
+
a^2 \sum_n c_n \mathcal O_n^{(4)}
+
O(a^4 k^6),
\label{eq:Leff_with_aniso_operators}
\end{equation}
where:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(\mathcal L_{U(1)}\) is the gauge-covariant infrared theory,
\item \(a\) is the microscopic lattice scale,
\item \(\mathcal O_n^{(4)}\) are local operators containing four derivatives
or two derivatives together with one field-strength insertion, etc.,
\item the coefficients \(c_n\) encode cubic-lattice effects.
\end{itemize}

The operators naturally fall into three classes:

\begin{enumerate}
\item operators that preserve both local \(U(1)\) and full rotational symmetry;
\item operators that preserve local \(U(1)\) but break full rotational symmetry
down to the cubic point group;
\item operators that explicitly break the local \(U(1)\) redundancy itself.
\end{enumerate}

Only the third class can generate a genuine gauge mass term at one loop.

\subsection{Class I: fully gauge-invariant and rotationally invariant operators}

The first class consists of operators built only from gauge-covariant objects
and contracted isotropically. Examples include
\begin{align}
\mathcal O^{(4)}_{1}
&=
(D^2\psi)^*(D^2\psi),
\\
\mathcal O^{(4)}_{2}
&=
(F_{\mu\nu}F^{\mu\nu})\,|\psi|^2,
\\
\mathcal O^{(4)}_{3}
&=
(\partial_\rho F_{\mu\nu})(\partial^\rho F^{\mu\nu}),
\\
\mathcal O^{(4)}_{4}
&=
\bigl[(D_\mu\psi)^*(D^\mu\psi)\bigr]^2 .
\end{align}
These operators preserve:
\begin{enumerate}
\item local \(U(1)\) redundancy,
\item rotational invariance,
\item transversality of the one-loop polarization tensor.
\end{enumerate}
Hence they cannot generate a Proca mass.

\subsection{Class II: cubic-anisotropic but \(U(1)\)-preserving operators}

The second class consists of operators that preserve local \(U(1)\) but break
continuous rotational invariance down to the cubic point group.
These are the most important operators for the BCC medium.

A convenient basis is:
\begin{align}
\mathcal O^{\mathrm{cub}}_{1}
&=
\sum_{i=x,y,z}
(D_i^2\psi)^*(D_i^2\psi),
\label{eq:Ocub1}
\\
\mathcal O^{\mathrm{cub}}_{2}
&=
\sum_{i=x,y,z}
\bigl[(D_i\psi)^*(D_i\psi)\bigr]^2,
\label{eq:Ocub2}
\\
\mathcal O^{\mathrm{cub}}_{3}
&=
\sum_{i=x,y,z}
F_{0i}F_{0i}
-
\frac13 \sum_{i,j=x,y,z}F_{0i}F_{0j},
\label{eq:Ocub3}
\\
\mathcal O^{\mathrm{cub}}_{4}
&=
\sum_{i=x,y,z}
F_{ij}F_{ij}
-
\frac13 \sum_{i,j,k,l=x,y,z}\delta_{ik}\delta_{jl}F_{ij}F_{kl},
\label{eq:Ocub4}
\\
\mathcal O^{\mathrm{cub}}_{5}
&=
\sum_{i=x,y,z}
(D_i\psi)^*(D_i\psi)\,|\psi|^2
-
\frac13 (D_j\psi)^*(D_j\psi)\,|\psi|^2.
\label{eq:Ocub5}
\end{align}

All of these operators satisfy the local transformation rule
\[
\psi \mapsto e^{i\alpha(x)}\psi,
\qquad
A_\mu \mapsto A_\mu + \frac{1}{q}\partial_\mu\alpha,
\]
and therefore remain gauge covariant or gauge invariant.
Their physical effect is to:
\begin{enumerate}
\item split the transverse velocities,
\item distort the polarization dispersion,
\item renormalize the gauge kinetic sector anisotropically,
\item possibly obstruct generic \(SO(2)\) degeneracy,
\end{enumerate}
but they do \emph{not} by themselves generate a gauge mass.
At one loop they preserve the Ward identity
\begin{equation}
p_\mu \Pi^{\mu\nu}(p)=0.
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Conclusion for Class II.}
Cubic anisotropy alone does not necessarily destroy the emergent gauge field.
It can deform the dispersion and break rotational symmetry, while still leaving
the gauge boson massless.

\subsection{Class III: \(U(1)\)-breaking operators}

The dangerous operators are those that are compatible with the microscopic
elastic medium but are not expressible in terms of the gauge-covariant bundle
structure. Representative examples are:
\begin{align}
\widetilde{\mathcal O}_{1}
&=
(\partial_i u_1)^2-(\partial_i u_2)^2,
\label{eq:O_break_1}
\\
\widetilde{\mathcal O}_{2}
&=
(\partial_i u_1)(\partial_j u_2)\,T^{ij},
\label{eq:O_break_2}
\\
\widetilde{\mathcal O}_{3}
&=
\psi^2 + \psi^{*2},
\label{eq:O_break_3}
\\
\widetilde{\mathcal O}_{4}
&=
A_\mu A^\mu,
\label{eq:O_break_4}
\\
\widetilde{\mathcal O}_{5}
&=
(\partial_i\psi)(\partial_i\psi)
+
(\partial_i\psi^*)(\partial_i\psi^*),
\label{eq:O_break_5}
\end{align}
where \(T^{ij}\) is a fixed cubic tensor.

These operators do one or more of the following:
\begin{enumerate}
\item distinguish \(u_1\) from \(u_2\) in a basis-dependent way,
\item violate local phase covariance,
\item reduce the symmetry from \(U(1)\) to a discrete subgroup,
\item directly generate a gauge-boson mass term,
\item spoil the Ward identity at loop level.
\end{enumerate}

For example, the operator \(\widetilde{\mathcal O}_4=A_\mu A^\mu\) is already
a Proca mass term. Likewise, \(\widetilde{\mathcal O}_3=\psi^2+\psi^{*2}\)
breaks \(U(1)\) down to \(\mathbb Z_2\), and its loop insertions can induce
non-transverse contributions to \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)\).

\paragraph{Conclusion for Class III.}
If any such operator is generated in the infrared EFT with a non-negligible
coefficient, then the emergent local \(U(1)\) interpretation fails and the
gauge field is not protected against mass generation.

\subsection{Basis-independent criterion}

The distinction above can be stated in a basis-independent way.

\paragraph{Criterion.}
An operator is admissible in the emergent gauge EFT if and only if it can be
written entirely in terms of the bundle-covariant objects
\[
\psi,\qquad D_\mu\psi,\qquad F_{\mu\nu},
\]
with all indices contracted by tensors compatible with the retained symmetry
(isotropic or cubic). Any operator that cannot be expressed in this form is an
explicit breaking of the local polarization-frame redundancy.

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O \text{ preserves emergent }U(1)
\iff
\mathcal O = \mathcal O(\psi,D_\mu\psi,F_{\mu\nu}).
}
\label{eq:covariant_operator_criterion}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Implication for the one-loop polarization tensor}

The one-loop gauge-mass test now becomes sharp.

\paragraph{If only Class I and Class II operators are present:}
the one-loop polarization tensor remains transverse,
\begin{equation}
p_\mu \Pi^{\mu\nu}(p)=0,
\end{equation}
and therefore
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0,
\qquad
m_A^2=0.
\end{equation}

\paragraph{If any Class III operator is present:}
then transversality may fail,
\begin{equation}
p_\mu \Pi^{\mu\nu}(p)\neq 0,
\end{equation}
and a nonzero gauge mass may appear:
\begin{equation}
m_A^2=\Pi_T(0)\neq 0.
\end{equation}

\subsection{Practical operator table}

For later use, we summarize the classification in table form.

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
Class & Example & Preserves local \(U(1)\)? & Can generate \(m_A^2\)? \\
\hline
I & \((D^2\psi)^*(D^2\psi)\) & Yes & No \\
I & \((\partial_\rho F_{\mu\nu})^2\) & Yes & No \\
II & \(\sum_i (D_i^2\psi)^*(D_i^2\psi)\) & Yes & No \\
II & cubic \(F_{\mu\nu}F_{\rho\sigma}\) contractions & Yes & No \\
III & \(\psi^2+\psi^{*2}\) & No & Yes \\
III & \(A_\mu A^\mu\) & No & Yes \\
III & basis-dependent \(u_1/u_2\) splitting terms & No & Yes \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\subsection{Final theorem}

We may summarize the classification as follows.

\paragraph{Theorem (anisotropy operator classification).}
The leading \(O(a^2k^4)\) lattice-induced operators in the BCC-condensed TECT
medium fall into three classes:

\begin{enumerate}
\item fully gauge-invariant and rotationally invariant operators;
\item cubic-anisotropic but gauge-invariant operators;
\item explicit \(U(1)\)-breaking operators.
\end{enumerate}

Only the third class can induce a gauge-field mass at one loop.
Therefore the one-loop survival of the emergent Maxwell sector is equivalent to
the statement that the infrared EFT contains only operators of Class I and II.

\subsection{Next computational step}

The remaining practical task is now explicit:
one must compute the insertion of each allowed Class II and candidate Class III
operator into the one-loop polarization tensor,
\[
\delta \mathcal L
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\delta \Pi_{\mu\nu}(p)
\;\Longrightarrow\;
\delta \Pi_{\mu\nu}(0),
\]
and verify whether
\[
\delta \Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
\delta \Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\neq 0.
\]

This is the precise quantum consistency test of the emergent \(U(1)\) gauge
structure in the lattice-corrected TECT theory.

\section{Explicit One-Loop Gauge-Mass Generation from a Soft \(U(1)\)-Breaking Operator}
\label{sec:explicit_classIII_mass_generation}

To make the one-loop gauge-mass test fully concrete, we now evaluate the effect
of a specific dangerous Class III operator.
The simplest choice is the soft \(U(1)\)-breaking term
\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{break}}
=
-\frac{\mu_B^2}{2}\left(\psi^2+\psi^{*2}\right).
\label{eq:soft_breaking_operator}
\end{equation}
This operator preserves reality and locality, but breaks the emergent
\(U(1)\) phase symmetry down to \(\mathbb Z_2\).

We show explicitly that, once \eqref{eq:soft_breaking_operator} is present,
the one-loop polarization tensor acquires a nonzero zero-momentum limit,
and therefore the emergent gauge field is no longer protected against a mass.

\subsection{Real-field decomposition}

Write the complex field in terms of real components:
\begin{equation}
\psi
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(\phi_1+i\phi_2),
\qquad
\psi^*
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}(\phi_1-i\phi_2).
\label{eq:psi_real_decomposition}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
|\psi|^2
=
\frac12(\phi_1^2+\phi_2^2),
\qquad
\psi^2+\psi^{*2}
=
\phi_1^2-\phi_2^2.
\label{eq:psi_breaking_real}
\end{equation}

Hence the soft-breaking term becomes
\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{break}}
=
-\frac{\mu_B^2}{2}\left(\phi_1^2-\phi_2^2\right).
\label{eq:breaking_term_real_basis}
\end{equation}
Therefore the two real transverse modes acquire different masses:
\begin{equation}
m_1^2 = m^2+\mu_B^2,
\qquad
m_2^2 = m^2-\mu_B^2.
\label{eq:mass_splitting_phi1_phi2}
\end{equation}

Thus the \(U(1)\) symmetry is explicitly broken by a mass splitting
between the two real transverse components.

\subsection{Gauge-coupled quadratic Lagrangian}

In the real basis, the minimal gauge-covariant kinetic term is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{kin}}
=
\frac12 (D_\mu \phi_a)(D^\mu \phi_a)
-
\frac12 m_a^2 \phi_a^2,
\qquad
a=1,2,
\label{eq:real_basis_kinetic}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
D_\mu
=
\partial_\mu + q A_\mu J,
\qquad
J=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & 1\\
-1 & 0
\end{pmatrix}.
\label{eq:real_basis_covariant_derivative}
\end{equation}
Expanding gives
\begin{align}
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{kin}}
&=
\frac12(\partial_\mu\phi_1)^2
+\frac12(\partial_\mu\phi_2)^2
-\frac12 m_1^2\phi_1^2
-\frac12 m_2^2\phi_2^2
\nonumber\\
&\qquad
+
q A_\mu
\left(
\phi_2 \partial^\mu \phi_1
-
\phi_1 \partial^\mu \phi_2
\right)
+
\frac{q^2}{2}A_\mu A^\mu (\phi_1^2+\phi_2^2).
\label{eq:expanded_real_basis_L}
\end{align}

Thus there are:

\begin{itemize}
\item an off-diagonal three-point vertex \(A\phi_1\phi_2\),
\item a diagonal seagull vertex \(AA\phi_a\phi_a\).
\end{itemize}

\subsection{One-loop polarization tensor at zero momentum}

At one loop, the gauge-field self-energy at zero external momentum receives:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a bubble contribution with one \(\phi_1\) propagator and one \(\phi_2\) propagator,
\item a seagull contribution from the \(A^2(\phi_1^2+\phi_2^2)\) term.
\end{enumerate}

Using Euclidean momentum notation for clarity, define
\begin{equation}
G_a(k)=\frac{1}{k^2+m_a^2},
\qquad a=1,2.
\label{eq:real_propagators}
\end{equation}
Then the zero-momentum polarization tensor takes the form
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
=
q^2
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
2\,k_\mu k_\nu\, G_1(k)G_2(k)
-
\delta_{\mu\nu}\,\frac{G_1(k)+G_2(k)}{2}
\right].
\label{eq:Pi_zero_real_basis}
\end{equation}

By rotational invariance of the loop measure,
\begin{equation}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d} \,
k_\mu k_\nu\, f(k^2)
=
\frac{\delta_{\mu\nu}}{d}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}\,
k^2 f(k^2).
\label{eq:rotational_identity_kmunu}
\end{equation}
Hence
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
=
q^2 \delta_{\mu\nu}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
\frac{2}{d}k^2 G_1(k)G_2(k)
-
\frac{G_1(k)+G_2(k)}{2}
\right].
\label{eq:Pi_zero_scalar_integral}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Check of the exact \(U(1)\) limit}

If \(m_1=m_2=m\), then \(G_1=G_2=G\), and
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
=
q^2 \delta_{\mu\nu}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
\frac{2}{d}k^2 G(k)^2 - G(k)
\right].
\label{eq:Pi_zero_equal_mass}
\end{equation}
In a gauge-invariant regularization scheme, the bubble and seagull parts cancel,
so that
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0.
\label{eq:Pi_zero_equal_mass_zero}
\end{equation}
This is the expected exact-\(U(1)\) result.

\subsection{Small symmetry-breaking expansion}

Now let
\begin{equation}
m_1^2 = m^2+\delta,
\qquad
m_2^2 = m^2-\delta,
\qquad
\delta=\mu_B^2.
\label{eq:delta_mass_def}
\end{equation}
Define
\begin{equation}
G(k)=\frac{1}{k^2+m^2}.
\end{equation}
Then for small \(\delta\),
\begin{align}
G_1(k)
&=
G - \delta G^2 + \delta^2 G^3 + O(\delta^3),
\\
G_2(k)
&=
G + \delta G^2 + \delta^2 G^3 + O(\delta^3),
\\
\frac{G_1+G_2}{2}
&=
G + \delta^2 G^3 + O(\delta^4),
\\
G_1 G_2
&=
G^2 + \delta^2 G^4 + O(\delta^4).
\label{eq:G1G2_expansion}
\end{align}
Substituting into \eqref{eq:Pi_zero_scalar_integral} gives
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
=
q^2 \delta_{\mu\nu}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
\frac{2}{d}k^2 G^2 - G
\right]
+
q^2\delta^2 \delta_{\mu\nu}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
\frac{2}{d}k^2 G^4 - G^3
\right]
+
O(\delta^4).
\label{eq:Pi_zero_delta_expand}
\end{equation}
The first bracket vanishes by the exact-\(U(1)\) cancellation
\eqref{eq:Pi_zero_equal_mass_zero}. Therefore
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
=
q^2 \delta^2 \delta_{\mu\nu}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
\frac{2}{d}k^2 G^4 - G^3
\right]
+
O(\delta^4).
\label{eq:Pi_zero_delta2}
\end{equation}

Using
\begin{equation}
k^2 G^4 = G^3 - m^2 G^4,
\label{eq:k2G4_identity}
\end{equation}
we obtain
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)
=
q^2 \delta^2 \delta_{\mu\nu}
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
\left(\frac{2}{d}-1\right)G^3
-
\frac{2m^2}{d}G^4
\right]
+
O(\delta^4).
\label{eq:Pi_zero_final_integrand}
\end{equation}
For generic \(d\) and \(m^2>0\), this coefficient is nonzero.
Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\neq 0
\qquad
\text{for}
\qquad
\delta=\mu_B^2\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:Pi_zero_nonzero_soft_breaking}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Induced gauge mass}

Since a zero-momentum polarization tensor of the form
\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=m_A^2 \delta_{\mu\nu}
\end{equation}
is precisely a Proca mass contribution, we conclude that the soft-breaking
operator \eqref{eq:soft_breaking_operator} induces a gauge mass at one loop:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
m_A^2
\sim
q^2 \mu_B^4
\int\frac{d^d k}{(2\pi)^d}
\left[
\left(\frac{2}{d}-1\right)G^3
-
\frac{2m^2}{d}G^4
\right]
+
O(\mu_B^8).
}
\label{eq:mA2_soft_breaking_result}
\end{equation}

Therefore the emergent gauge boson is no longer protected once the local
\(U(1)\) redundancy is explicitly broken.

\subsection{Interpretation}

This calculation gives a concrete realization of the general operator
classification:

\begin{enumerate}
\item If the infrared EFT contains only Class I and Class II operators,
then \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0\) and the emergent gauge field remains massless.
\item If a Class III operator such as
\[
-\frac{\mu_B^2}{2}(\psi^2+\psi^{*2})
\]
is present, then the one-loop polarization tensor develops a nonzero
zero-momentum limit and a gauge mass is generated.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the one-loop mass test is not merely formal; it explicitly distinguishes
between a true emergent gauge symmetry and an approximate phase structure.

\subsection{Final theorem}

\paragraph{Theorem.}
Consider the transverse EFT coupled to the emergent connection \(A_\mu\).
If one adds the soft \(U(1)\)-breaking operator
\[
\delta \mathcal L_{\mathrm{break}}
=
-\frac{\mu_B^2}{2}(\psi^2+\psi^{*2}),
\]
then the two real transverse modes acquire unequal masses
\[
m_1^2=m^2+\mu_B^2,
\qquad
m_2^2=m^2-\mu_B^2,
\]
and the one-loop gauge polarization tensor satisfies
\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\neq 0.
\]
Consequently the emergent gauge field acquires a nonzero mass
\[
m_A^2\neq 0.
\]
Hence this operator destroys the exact emergent Maxwell sector.

\paragraph{Corollary.}
The survival of the massless emergent gauge field requires the absence of all
Class III operators from the infrared EFT.

\section{Classification of \(O(a^2k^4)\) Anisotropy Operators}
\label{sec:classification_a2k4}

In this section, we classify the leading lattice-induced \(O(a^2k^4)\) operators
in the BCC-condensed TECT framework and determine whether they preserve the local
\(U(1)\) symmetry or break it.

\subsection{Class II: \(U(1)\) preserving, rotationally anisotropic operators}

The Class II operators are those that preserve the local \(U(1)\) redundancy
but break full rotational symmetry, leaving only the cubic point group. These
operators involve four derivatives and are invariant under the cubic lattice symmetry.
Examples include:

\begin{itemize}
\item \( \mathcal O^{\mathrm{cub}}_1 = (D_i^2\psi)^*(D_i^2\psi) \),
\item \( \mathcal O^{\mathrm{cub}}_2 = \big[(D_i \psi)^*(D_i \psi)\big]^2 \),
\item \( \mathcal O^{\mathrm{cub}}_3 = \sum_i F_{0i}F_{0i} - \frac13 \sum_{i,j=x,y,z} F_{0i} F_{0j} \).
\end{itemize}

These operators do not break the \(U(1)\) symmetry, and they do not generate a mass for the gauge field at one loop. Therefore, they preserve the gauge field's massless nature.

\subsection{Class III: \(U(1)\) breaking operators}

Class III operators break the local \(U(1)\) symmetry and can generate a mass for the gauge field.
Examples include:

\begin{itemize}
\item \( \mathcal O^{\mathrm{break}}_3 = \psi^2 + \psi^{*2} \),
\item \( \mathcal O^{\mathrm{break}}_4 = A_\mu A^\mu \).
\end{itemize}

These operators break the \(U(1)\) symmetry and generate a mass for the gauge field. Therefore, if any of these operators are present in the effective theory, the emergent gauge boson will acquire a mass at one loop.

\subsection{Operator classification summary}

The leading lattice-induced \(O(a^2k^4)\) operators in the BCC-condensed TECT medium
fall into three classes:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Class II: Fully gauge-invariant and rotationally invariant operators that do not generate a gauge mass,
\item Class III: \(U(1)\)-breaking operators that generate a gauge mass at one loop.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Next computational step}

The next step is to classify the lattice-induced operators in the EFT and evaluate their contribution
to the one-loop polarization tensor, \(\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)\). We must verify whether:

\begin{equation}
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0) = 0
\qquad \text{or} \qquad
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0) \neq 0.
\end{equation}

This will determine whether the emergent gauge field remains massless or acquires a mass.

\section{Microscopic Origin of the Leading \(O(a^2k^4)\) Lattice Operators}
\label{sec:microscopic_origin_a2k4}

We now determine whether the leading lattice-induced \(O(a^2k^4)\) corrections
generated by the BCC condensate belong to Class II (gauge-preserving anisotropy)
or Class III (explicit \(U(1)\)-breaking operators).

The key point is that these operators must be derived from the microscopic
real displacement field before introducing the complex transverse coordinate.
This allows one to distinguish genuine lattice anisotropy from basis-dependent
phase-breaking terms.

\subsection{Microscopic elastic expansion beyond leading order}

At long wavelength, the quadratic elastic kernel may be expanded as
\begin{equation}
D_{ij}(\mathbf k)
=
D^{(2)}_{ij}(\mathbf k)
+
D^{(4)}_{ij}(\mathbf k)
+
O(a^4k^6),
\label{eq:D_expand_2_4}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
D^{(2)}_{ij}(\mathbf k)=C_{ijkl}k_k k_l
\end{equation}
is the leading cubic elastic kernel, and
\begin{equation}
D^{(4)}_{ij}(\mathbf k)=a^2\, \mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)
\end{equation}
collects the leading higher-gradient lattice corrections.

Because the microscopic BCC condensate is real and invariant under the cubic
point group, inversion, and translations, the tensor
\(\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)\) must satisfy:

\begin{enumerate}
\item reality:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)\in \mathbb R,
\end{equation}
\item evenness in momentum:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}(-\mathbf k)=\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}(\mathbf k),
\end{equation}
\item cubic covariance,
\item symmetry in the vector indices:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}=\mathcal M_{ji}^{(4)}.
\end{equation}
\end{enumerate}

Thus the most general leading correction is a real symmetric cubic-covariant
rank-two tensor homogeneous of degree four in \(\mathbf k\).

\subsection{General cubic form of the \(O(a^2k^4)\) kernel}

By cubic symmetry, the most general such tensor can be written as a linear
combination of the following structures:
\begin{align}
\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)
&=
\alpha_1\, k^4 \delta_{ij}
+\alpha_2\, k^2 k_i k_j
+\alpha_3\, \delta_{ij}\sum_{\ell}k_\ell^4
+\alpha_4\, k_i k_j (k_i^2+k_j^2)
\nonumber\\
&\qquad
+\alpha_5\, \delta_{ij}k_i^2 k^2
+\alpha_6\, \delta_{ij}k_i^4
+\alpha_7\, (1-\delta_{ij})\,k_i k_j k^2
+\cdots,
\label{eq:general_cubic_k4_tensor}
\end{align}
where the omitted terms are not independent after symmetrization and cubic
reduction.

The important point is that \(\mathcal M_{ij}^{(4)}\) is a tensor acting on the
\emph{real} displacement vector \(\mathbf u\). It contains no reference to a
chosen transverse basis \((u_1,u_2)\), and therefore no explicit phase angle
appears at the microscopic level.

\subsection{Projection to the transverse subspace}

Let
\begin{equation}
P^T_{ij}(\mathbf k)=\delta_{ij}-\frac{k_i k_j}{k^2}
\end{equation}
be the transverse projector. The projected transverse correction is
\begin{equation}
\mathcal M^{(4)}_{T}(\mathbf k)
=
P^T(\mathbf k)\,\mathcal M^{(4)}(\mathbf k)\,P^T(\mathbf k),
\label{eq:transverse_projected_M4}
\end{equation}
which acts on the two-dimensional transverse subspace \(\hat{\mathbf k}^\perp\).

Choose an orthonormal transverse frame \(e_a^i(\mathbf k)\), \(a=1,2\), and define
the \(2\times 2\) projected kernel
\begin{equation}
K_{ab}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)
=
e_a^i(\mathbf k)\,
\mathcal M^{(4)}_{ij}(\mathbf k)\,
e_b^j(\mathbf k).
\label{eq:Kab_projected}
\end{equation}
Since \(\mathcal M^{(4)}_{ij}\) is real symmetric, \(K_{ab}^{(4)}\) is also
real symmetric:
\begin{equation}
K_{ab}^{(4)} = K_{ba}^{(4)}.
\end{equation}

Any real symmetric \(2\times 2\) matrix can be decomposed as
\begin{equation}
K^{(4)}(\mathbf k)
=
\kappa_0(\mathbf k)\,\mathbf 1_2
+
\kappa_1(\mathbf k)\,\sigma_3
+
\kappa_2(\mathbf k)\,\sigma_1,
\label{eq:K2x2_decomposition}
\end{equation}
where \(\sigma_1,\sigma_3\) are Pauli matrices in the transverse polarization
basis.

\subsection{Class II vs Class III criterion in the projected transverse plane}

The decomposition \eqref{eq:K2x2_decomposition} has a direct interpretation.

\paragraph{Class II.}
If
\begin{equation}
K^{(4)}(\mathbf k)=\kappa_0(\mathbf k)\,\mathbf 1_2,
\label{eq:classII_condition}
\end{equation}
then the correction is proportional to the identity on the transverse plane.
It does not distinguish \(u_1\) from \(u_2\), and therefore preserves the
local phase redundancy. In complex notation it can be written covariantly as
\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal L^{(4)}_{T}
\sim
a^2 \kappa_0(\mathbf k)\,\psi^*\psi,
\end{equation}
or, after promoting derivatives to covariant derivatives,
\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal L^{(4)}_{T}
\sim
a^2\,(D_i^2\psi)^*(D_i^2\psi),
\end{equation}
which is Class II.

\paragraph{Class III.}
If either
\begin{equation}
\kappa_1(\mathbf k)\neq 0
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
\kappa_2(\mathbf k)\neq 0,
\label{eq:classIII_condition}
\end{equation}
then the correction distinguishes directions in the transverse plane.
In a chosen basis this gives terms of the form
\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal L^{(4)}_{T}
\sim
a^2\left[
\kappa_1(u_1^2-u_2^2)
+
2\kappa_2 u_1 u_2
\right],
\label{eq:classIII_basis_split}
\end{equation}
or equivalently, in complex notation,
\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal L^{(4)}_{T}
\sim
a^2\left[
\tilde\kappa(\mathbf k)\,\psi^2
+
\tilde\kappa^*(\mathbf k)\,\psi^{*2}
\right].
\label{eq:classIII_psi2_form}
\end{equation}
These are explicit \(U(1)\)-breaking structures, i.e. Class III.

Thus the microscopic question reduces to whether the transverse projection
\eqref{eq:Kab_projected} is proportional to the identity or not.

\subsection{Exact result in symmetry-degenerate channels}

We now evaluate the structure of \(K_{ab}^{(4)}\) along the symmetry directions
where the leading transverse sector is already exactly degenerate.

\subsubsection*{Channel \([100]\)}

Let
\begin{equation}
\mathbf k = (k,0,0).
\end{equation}
Then the transverse plane is spanned by
\begin{equation}
\mathbf e_1=(0,1,0),
\qquad
\mathbf e_2=(0,0,1).
\end{equation}
By cubic symmetry, the \(y\) and \(z\) directions are equivalent.
Therefore
\begin{equation}
K_{ab}^{(4)}([100])
=
\kappa_{[100]}(k)\,\delta_{ab}.
\label{eq:K_100_identity}
\end{equation}
Hence the leading \(O(a^2k^4)\) correction is proportional to the identity on
the transverse plane and is therefore Class II.

\subsubsection*{Channel \([111]\)}

Let
\begin{equation}
\mathbf k=\frac{k}{\sqrt3}(1,1,1).
\end{equation}
The little group of \([111]\) contains a threefold rotation that cyclically
permutes the coordinates. On the transverse plane, this symmetry acts
irreducibly and forbids any preferred transverse axis. Therefore the projected
kernel must again be proportional to the identity:
\begin{equation}
K_{ab}^{(4)}([111])
=
\kappa_{[111]}(k)\,\delta_{ab}.
\label{eq:K_111_identity}
\end{equation}
Thus the leading \(O(a^2k^4)\) correction is again Class II.

\paragraph{Conclusion.}
In the exactly degenerate symmetry channels \([100]\) and \([111]\), the
microscopic BCC lattice generates leading \(O(a^2k^4)\) corrections that are
Class II, not Class III.

\subsection{Generic directions}

For a generic propagation direction \(\mathbf k\), the little group is smaller,
and cubic symmetry no longer forces the projected kernel \(K_{ab}^{(4)}\) to
be proportional to the identity. In general one may have
\begin{equation}
K^{(4)}(\mathbf k)
=
\kappa_0(\mathbf k)\,\mathbf 1_2
+
\kappa_1(\mathbf k)\,\sigma_3
+
\kappa_2(\mathbf k)\,\sigma_1.
\label{eq:generic_K4_projected}
\end{equation}
Therefore:

\begin{itemize}
\item if \(\kappa_1=\kappa_2=0\), the correction is Class II;
\item if either \(\kappa_1\) or \(\kappa_2\) is nonzero, the correction is Class III.
\end{itemize}

This is precisely the point at which infrared isotropization or an exact RG
argument becomes necessary for a fully generic \(U(1)\) statement.

\subsection{Microscopic theorem}

We may summarize the result as follows.

\paragraph{Theorem.}
Let \(D^{(4)}_{ij}(\mathbf k)=a^2\mathcal M^{(4)}_{ij}(\mathbf k)\) be the leading
higher-gradient correction induced by the microscopic BCC condensate.

\begin{enumerate}
\item The correction is a real symmetric cubic-covariant tensor acting on the
real displacement field.
\item After projection to the transverse plane, it defines a real symmetric
\(2\times 2\) kernel \(K_{ab}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)\).
\item In the symmetry-degenerate channels \([100]\) and \([111]\), one has
\[
K_{ab}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)=\kappa(\mathbf k)\delta_{ab},
\]
so the leading \(O(a^2k^4)\) correction is Class II.
\item For generic \(\mathbf k\), the traceless part of \(K_{ab}^{(4)}\) may
be nonzero, so a Class III contribution is not excluded without an additional
infrared isotropization argument.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the microscopic BCC lattice does \emph{not} force \(U(1)\)-breaking
at leading order in the symmetry-degenerate channels, but the fully generic
case remains conditional.

\subsection{Practical implication for the one-loop mass test}

This result has a direct consequence for the quantum consistency problem:

\begin{itemize}
\item In the \([100]\) and \([111]\) channels, the leading lattice correction is
Class II, hence it cannot generate a gauge mass at one loop.
\item For generic directions, one must determine whether the projected traceless
piece \((\kappa_1,\kappa_2)\) survives in the infrared.
\end{itemize}

Thus the next remaining open problem is sharply identified:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Does the traceless transverse projection of }D^{(4)}_{ij}(\mathbf k)
\text{ flow to zero for generic } \mathbf k \text{ in the deep IR?}
}
\end{equation}

If yes, then the generic emergent \(U(1)\) survives. If not, then the gauge
sector is exact only in the symmetry-degenerate channels.

\section{BCC as the Unique Resonance-Geometry Extremizer}
\label{sec:BCC_resonance_extremizer}

In this section, we present the complete mathematical proof that the BCC star is the unique maximizer of the sharp resonance functional within the admissible class of single-shell star geometries. This result is based on the previous classification of resonance geometries and proves that the BCC structure is the only geometry that maximizes the resonance functional.

\subsection{Admissible Single-Shell Stars}

Let \(S \subset q_0 S^2 \subset \mathbb{R}^3\) be an admissible centrosymmetric single-shell star, as defined in the previous section. The star \(S\) consists of a set of \(m\) points \( \{\pm Q_1, \dots, \pm Q_m\} \), where \( |Q_a| = q_0 \) for each \( Q_a \), and these points satisfy the appropriate geometric conditions for resonance selection, including centrosymmetry, single-shell support, and quartic/sextic resonance constraints.

\subsection{The Sharp Resonance Functional}

Let \( \mathcal{R}[S] \) denote the sharp resonance functional, as defined and classified earlier in the manuscript. Depending on the normalization adopted in the previous sections, this functional may correspond to one of the following:

\begin{itemize}
    \item The pure quartic resonance functional,
    \item The quartic resonance functional with trivial self-contractions removed,
    \item The combined quartic and sextic resonance functional relevant for the effective structural-selection problem.
\end{itemize}

For the purposes of this theorem, we only need the following result from the sharp resonance classification theorem:

\paragraph{Sharp Resonance Classification Theorem.}
For any admissible single-shell star \( S \),
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{R}[S] \le \mathcal{R}[S_{\mathrm{BCC}}],
\label{eq:sharp_resonance_bound}
\end{equation}
with equality if and only if \( S \) is congruent to the BCC star:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{R}[S] = \mathcal{R}[S_{\mathrm{BCC}}] \iff S \cong S_{\mathrm{BCC}}.
\label{eq:sharp_resonance_equality}
\end{equation}

\subsection{Normalized Extremality Coefficient}

Let \( C_S \) denote the normalized extremality coefficient of \( S \), defined as the ratio of the sharp resonance functional of \( S \) to that of the BCC star:
\begin{equation}
C_S := \frac{\mathcal{R}[S]}{\mathcal{R}[S_{\mathrm{BCC}}]}.
\label{eq:CS_definition}
\end{equation}
Since \( \mathcal{R}[S_{\mathrm{BCC}}] > 0 \), this coefficient is well-defined for all admissible stars.

\subsection{Main Theorem}

\paragraph{Theorem (BCC as the Unique Resonance-Geometry Extremizer).}
For every admissible single-shell centrosymmetric star \( S \),
\begin{equation}
C_S \le 1,
\label{eq:CS_le_1}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
C_S = 1 \iff S \cong S_{\mathrm{BCC}}.
\label{eq:CS_eq_1_iff_BCC}
\end{equation}
Thus, the BCC star is the unique resonance-geometry extremizer.

\paragraph{Proof.}
By the definition of \( C_S \), we have
\[
C_S = \frac{\mathcal{R}[S]}{\mathcal{R}[S_{\mathrm{BCC}}]}.
\]
Using the sharp resonance bound \eqref{eq:sharp_resonance_bound}, we obtain
\[
C_S = \frac{\mathcal{R}[S]}{\mathcal{R}[S_{\mathrm{BCC}}]} \le 1.
\]
This proves inequality \eqref{eq:CS_le_1}.

For the equality statement, observe that
\[
C_S = 1 \iff \mathcal{R}[S] = \mathcal{R}[S_{\mathrm{BCC}}].
\]
By the uniqueness part of the sharp classification theorem, \eqref{eq:sharp_resonance_equality}, this equality holds if and only if \( S \cong S_{\mathrm{BCC}} \). Therefore, we conclude that
\[
C_S = 1 \iff S \cong S_{\mathrm{BCC}}.
\]
This proves \eqref{eq:CS_eq_1_iff_BCC}. \(\boxed{\text{Q.E.D.}}\)

\subsection{Interpretation}

The significance of this theorem is that the BCC star is not merely preferred
among a finite set of candidates; rather, it is the unique maximizer of the
sharp resonance functional within the entire admissible class of single-shell
geometries. The inequality
\[
C_S \le 1
\]
establishes a universal bound, and the saturation point
\[
C_S = 1
\]
is realized uniquely by the BCC star. This demonstrates that the BCC star is
intrinsically characterized by resonance saturation, rather than being a merely
energetically favored candidate among a small set of possible geometries.

\subsection{Corollary: Structural Rigidity of the Extremizer}

\paragraph{Corollary.}
If \( S \) is not congruent to the BCC star, then
\begin{equation}
C_S < 1.
\label{eq:strict_gap_nonBCC}
\end{equation}
Moreover, if the admissible class is discrete up to rotation and scaling, there
exists a strict gap \( \delta > 0 \) such that
\begin{equation}
S \not\cong S_{\mathrm{BCC}} \quad \Longrightarrow \quad C_S \le 1 - \delta.
\label{eq:uniform_gap_delta}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{Proof.}
The strict inequality \eqref{eq:strict_gap_nonBCC} follows immediately from
the uniqueness statement \eqref{eq:CS_eq_1_iff_BCC}. If the admissible class
is discrete modulo Euclidean congruence, then the set of values
\[
\{ C_S : S \text{ admissible},\ S \not\cong S_{\mathrm{BCC}} \}
\]
has a maximum strictly below 1. Writing that maximum as \( 1 - \delta \)
proves \eqref{eq:uniform_gap_delta}. \(\boxed{\text{Q.E.D.}}\)

\subsection{Corollary: Resonance-Geometric Form of BCC Selection}

The structural-selection problem can therefore be restated geometrically:

\paragraph{Corollary.}
Any effective free-energy criterion that is monotone increasing in \( \mathcal{R}[S] \)
selects the BCC star uniquely within the admissible single-shell class.

\paragraph{Proof.}
Since \( \mathcal{R}[S] \) is uniquely maximized by \( S_{\mathrm{BCC}} \), any
strictly monotone function of \( \mathcal{R}[S] \) is also uniquely extremized at
\( S_{\mathrm{BCC}} \). \(\boxed{\text{Q.E.D.}}\)

\subsection{Practical Insertion into the Main Text}

For compactness, the result can be stated succinctly as follows:

\[
\boxed{
C_S \le 1,
\qquad
C_S = 1 \iff S \cong S_{\mathrm{BCC}}.
}
\]

This is the precise mathematical statement that the BCC star is the unique resonance-geometry extremizer.

\section{Summary of Mathematical Status}
\label{sec:status}

For clarity we summarize which results of the present work are
fully established theorems, which are conditional statements,
and which remain open problems.

\subsection{Rigorous Theorems}

The following results are proven within the framework developed
in this work.

\paragraph{Theorem 1 (Longitudinal decoupling).}

If the volumetric sector possesses a mass gap \(M_L\),
then for energies

\[
\omega \ll M_L
\]

the longitudinal displacement mode decouples from the
transverse sector. The low–energy effective theory therefore
contains only the transverse modes.

\paragraph{Theorem 2 (Transverse bundle structure).}

For a three–dimensional elastic medium the displacement field
satisfies

\[
k_i u_i = 0
\]

in the transverse sector. Therefore the transverse degrees of
freedom form a two–dimensional bundle over momentum space.

\paragraph{Theorem 3 (Local frame redundancy).}

The choice of transverse polarization basis
\((e_1,e_2)\) is not unique.
Local rotations

\[
(e_1,e_2)\rightarrow
(\cos\theta\,e_1+\sin\theta\,e_2,
-\sin\theta\,e_1+\cos\theta\,e_2)
\]

define a local \(SO(2)\) gauge redundancy.

\paragraph{Theorem 4 (Emergent connection).}

The momentum–dependent polarization frame defines a
connection

\[
A_\mu = i\, e_a^\dagger \partial_\mu e_a
\]

which transforms as a \(U(1)\) gauge field under
local frame rotations.

\paragraph{Theorem 5 (Curvature).}

The associated field strength is

\[
F_{\mu\nu} =
\partial_\mu A_\nu
-
\partial_\nu A_\mu .
\]

\paragraph{Theorem 6 (BCC resonance extremizer).}

For every admissible single–shell star \(S\)

\[
C_S \le 1,
\]

and

\[
C_S = 1 \iff S \cong S_{\mathrm{BCC}}.
\]

Hence the BCC star uniquely maximizes the resonance
functional.

\subsection{Conditional Theorems}

The following statements hold provided additional
conditions on the effective operators are satisfied.

\paragraph{Conditional Theorem (Massless emergent gauge sector).}

If the infrared effective action contains only operators
constructed from the gauge–covariant objects

\[
\psi,\quad D_\mu\psi,\quad F_{\mu\nu},
\]

then the one–loop polarization tensor satisfies

\[
\Pi_{\mu\nu}(0)=0.
\]

Therefore the emergent gauge boson remains massless.

\paragraph{Conditional Theorem (Symmetry directions).}

For propagation directions

\[
[100],\quad [111],
\]

the leading lattice correction satisfies

\[
K_{ab}^{(4)}(\mathbf k)
\propto
\delta_{ab},
\]

so that the \(O(a^2k^4)\) correction belongs to Class II.

\subsection{Open Problems}

The following problems remain open.

\paragraph{Open Problem 1 (Generic momentum directions).}

For a generic momentum direction

\[
\mathbf k,
\]

the projected kernel may contain a traceless component

\[
K^{(4)}_{ab}
=
\kappa_0 I
+
\kappa_1\sigma_3
+
\kappa_2\sigma_1 .
\]

Whether

\[
\kappa_1,\kappa_2\rightarrow 0
\quad
(k\rightarrow 0)
\]

holds universally remains to be proven.

\paragraph{Open Problem 2 (Infrared isotropization).}

A full renormalization–group proof that cubic anisotropy
flows to zero in the infrared is not yet established in the
present work.

Such a proof would upgrade the conditional Maxwell sector
to a fully rigorous theorem.

\section{Global \(U(1)\) Bundle Structure and Topological Obstructions}
\label{sec:global_bundle_structure}

The local polarization-frame redundancy of the transverse sector naturally
defines a local \(U(1)\) connection. However, local gauge data do not by
themselves determine the global topology of the emergent bundle.
A separate analysis is required to determine whether the local connection
extends to a globally trivial \(U(1)\) bundle or whether nontrivial
topological obstructions are possible.

\subsection{Current status}

At the present stage of the theory, only the \emph{local} bundle structure
has been established. More precisely, on each local patch \(U_\alpha\),
one may choose a transverse polarization frame and define a complex field
\(\psi_\alpha\) together with a local connection \(A_\alpha\).
On overlaps \(U_\alpha\cap U_\beta\), these transform as
\begin{equation}
\psi_\alpha = e^{i\chi_{\alpha\beta}} \psi_\beta,
\qquad
A_\alpha = A_\beta + \frac{1}{q}\, d\chi_{\alpha\beta}.
\label{eq:local_patch_transition}
\end{equation}
This is exactly the local transformation law of a \(U(1)\) connection.

What has \emph{not} yet been completed is the global analysis:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the identification of the correct base manifold \(M\),
\item the explicit construction of an atlas \(\{U_\alpha\}\),
\item the determination of transition functions \(g_{\alpha\beta}=e^{i\chi_{\alpha\beta}}\),
\item the cocycle test on triple overlaps,
\item the classification of possible first Chern classes.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Required global construction}

To promote the local data to a genuine global \(U(1)\) bundle, one must specify:

\paragraph{(i) The base manifold.}
Depending on the formulation, the relevant base manifold may be:
\begin{itemize}
\item a region of real space \(M\subset \mathbb R^3\) with defects removed,
\item a spacetime region \(M\subset \mathbb R^{3,1}\),
\item or a momentum-space manifold such as \(S^2\) of propagation directions.
\end{itemize}
The topology of \(M\) determines whether nontrivial bundle classes are even possible.

\paragraph{(ii) Local trivializations.}
Choose an open cover \(\{U_\alpha\}\) of \(M\). On each patch, pick a local
orthonormal transverse frame \((e_1^{(\alpha)},e_2^{(\alpha)})\) and define
the local complex field \(\psi_\alpha\).

\paragraph{(iii) Transition functions.}
On overlaps \(U_\alpha\cap U_\beta\), the two local frames differ by a local
rotation:
\begin{equation}
\psi_\alpha = g_{\alpha\beta}\,\psi_\beta,
\qquad
g_{\alpha\beta}: U_\alpha\cap U_\beta \to U(1),
\qquad
g_{\alpha\beta}=e^{i\chi_{\alpha\beta}}.
\label{eq:transition_function_def}
\end{equation}

\paragraph{(iv) Cocycle condition.}
On triple overlaps \(U_\alpha\cap U_\beta\cap U_\gamma\), consistency requires
\begin{equation}
g_{\alpha\beta}g_{\beta\gamma}g_{\gamma\alpha}=1,
\label{eq:cocycle_condition}
\end{equation}
or equivalently
\begin{equation}
\chi_{\alpha\beta}+\chi_{\beta\gamma}+\chi_{\gamma\alpha}\in 2\pi\mathbb Z.
\label{eq:phase_cocycle_condition}
\end{equation}
This is the condition for the local data to define a global \(U(1)\) bundle.

\subsection{First Chern class}

Once the transition functions are fixed, the topological class of the bundle
is measured by the first Chern class
\begin{equation}
c_1(P)=\frac{1}{2\pi}[F]\in H^2(M,\mathbb Z),
\label{eq:first_chern_class}
\end{equation}
where \(F=dA_\alpha\) on each local patch.
This is well-defined because \(F\) is gauge invariant and patch-independent.

The key global question is whether \(c_1(P)\) can be nonzero.
If
\begin{equation}
c_1(P)=0,
\end{equation}
then the emergent \(U(1)\) bundle is globally trivial and the local complex-field
description extends globally without obstruction.
If instead
\begin{equation}
c_1(P)\neq 0,
\end{equation}
then the theory admits topologically nontrivial sectors, such as quantized flux,
defect-supported holonomy, or monopole-like singular structures.

\subsection{Defects and punctured manifolds}

If the condensed TECT medium contains holes, line defects, or singular
polarization textures, then the relevant base manifold is not simply connected.
For example:
\begin{itemize}
\item removing a line defect produces nontrivial \(\pi_1(M)\), allowing
nontrivial holonomy around loops,
\item removing a point defect in three dimensions may lead to nontrivial
\(H^2(M,\mathbb Z)\), allowing nonzero \(c_1\),
\item momentum-space singularities may likewise induce nontrivial bundle
topology over direction space.
\end{itemize}

Thus, even if the local emergent \(U(1)\) connection is perfectly regular,
global nontriviality may still arise from the topology of the underlying
manifold or defect structure.

\subsection{What remains to be done}

The following global questions remain open in the present work:

\begin{enumerate}
\item What is the correct base manifold \(M\) for the polarization bundle in
the TECT setting?
\item Are the transition functions \(g_{\alpha\beta}\) globally trivializable,
or do defects force nontrivial patching?
\item Can the first Chern class \(c_1(P)\) be nonzero?
\item If \(c_1(P)\neq 0\), what is the physical interpretation of the associated
flux or holonomy in the condensed TECT medium?
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Precise status statement}

Therefore the mathematically correct status is:

\begin{quote}
The local \(U(1)\) spin connection of the transverse polarization bundle has
been established, but the global bundle topology --- including explicit
transition functions, possible nontrivial holonomy, and the first Chern class ---
has not yet been fully analyzed.
\end{quote}

This remains an important open problem for the global completion of the
emergent gauge structure.

\section{Global Topology of the Transverse Polarization Bundle}
\label{sec:global_topology}

The transverse sector of the elastic medium possesses a local
\(U(1)\) redundancy associated with rotations of the polarization
frame. While the local structure is clear, the global topology
of the associated bundle must be analyzed separately.

In particular we must determine the base manifold of the bundle
and whether the resulting \(U(1)\) bundle admits a nontrivial
first Chern class.

\subsection{Candidate base manifolds}

Two different manifolds arise naturally in the present framework.

\paragraph{Real-space manifold}

If the polarization field is viewed as a field over physical
space, the base manifold is

\[
M=\mathbb R^3.
\]

Since

\[
H^2(\mathbb R^3)=0,
\]

every \(U(1)\) bundle over \(\mathbb R^3\) is topologically trivial.

Therefore

\[
c_1(P)=0.
\]

Hence the polarization bundle is globally trivial in real space,
provided the medium contains no topological defects.

\paragraph{Direction-space manifold}

However, the polarization frame is defined relative to the
propagation direction

\[
\hat{\mathbf k}=\frac{\mathbf k}{|\mathbf k|}.
\]

Therefore the natural base manifold for the polarization bundle
is the sphere of directions

\[
M=S^2.
\]

This space has nontrivial second cohomology:

\[
H^2(S^2,\mathbb Z)=\mathbb Z.
\]

Consequently nontrivial \(U(1)\) bundles are possible.

\subsection{Polarization bundle over \(S^2\)}

For each direction \(\hat{\mathbf k}\in S^2\),
the transverse polarization space is

\[
T_{\hat{\mathbf k}} =
\{\mathbf u\in\mathbb R^3 \mid
\hat{\mathbf k}\cdot\mathbf u = 0\}.
\]

Choosing a local orthonormal basis

\[
(e_1(\hat{\mathbf k}), e_2(\hat{\mathbf k}))
\]

defines a local complex polarization coordinate

\[
\psi = u_1 + i u_2.
\]

Changing the transverse basis by a local rotation

\[
(e_1,e_2)\rightarrow
(\cos\theta\,e_1+\sin\theta\,e_2,
-\sin\theta\,e_1+\cos\theta\,e_2)
\]

induces the transformation

\[
\psi\rightarrow e^{i\theta}\psi.
\]

Thus the transverse bundle defines a \(U(1)\) principal bundle
over \(S^2\).

\subsection{Connection and curvature}

The local polarization frame defines a connection

\[
A_i = i e_a^\dagger \partial_i e_a .
\]

The curvature is

\[
F_{ij} =
\partial_i A_j -
\partial_j A_i.
\]

The topological class of the bundle is given by

\[
c_1 =
\frac{1}{2\pi}
\int_{S^2} F .
\]

\subsection{Monopole structure}

For the polarization bundle the curvature takes the form

\[
F =
q_m
\sin\theta\, d\theta \wedge d\phi .
\]

Therefore

\[
c_1 =
\frac{1}{2\pi}
\int_{S^2}
q_m \sin\theta\, d\theta d\phi
=
2 q_m .
\]

Thus the bundle corresponds to a Dirac monopole of charge \(q_m\).

\subsection{Physical interpretation}

The transverse polarization bundle is therefore
topologically equivalent to the Berry bundle of photon
polarization.

The nontrivial topology arises not from real-space defects
but from the geometry of the sphere of propagation directions.

\subsection{Theorem}

\paragraph{Theorem (Global polarization bundle).}

The transverse polarization bundle in the condensed
TECT elastic medium has the following topology:

\begin{enumerate}

\item
Over real space \(M=\mathbb R^3\), the bundle is trivial.

\item
Over the direction sphere \(M=S^2\), the bundle may possess
a nontrivial first Chern class

\[
c_1 \in H^2(S^2,\mathbb Z)=\mathbb Z.
\]

\end{enumerate}

Thus the emergent \(U(1)\) gauge structure admits
a monopole-type topological sector in direction space.

\subsection{Implication}

This structure implies that the emergent gauge field
is naturally associated with a Berry-type connection
on the sphere of propagation directions.

The global topology therefore matches that of the
standard photon polarization bundle.

\section{Emergence of Spin-1 Helicity}
\label{sec:spin1_helicity}

We now show that the transverse polarization bundle
naturally produces helicity \(\pm1\) modes.

\subsection{Transverse degrees of freedom}

For each propagation direction

\[
\hat{\mathbf k}=\frac{\mathbf k}{|\mathbf k|},
\]

the displacement field satisfies

\[
\hat{\mathbf k}\cdot \mathbf u = 0.
\]

Thus the polarization space is two-dimensional:

\[
T_{\hat{\mathbf k}}
=
\{\mathbf u\in\mathbb R^3\mid
\hat{\mathbf k}\cdot \mathbf u=0\}.
\]

Choose an orthonormal frame

\[
(e_1(\hat{\mathbf k}),e_2(\hat{\mathbf k})).
\]

The displacement field decomposes as

\[
\mathbf u
=
u_1 e_1 + u_2 e_2.
\]

\subsection{Complex polarization coordinate}

Define

\[
\psi = u_1 + i u_2 .
\]

A rotation of the transverse frame

\[
(e_1,e_2)
\rightarrow
(\cos\theta\,e_1+\sin\theta\,e_2,
-\sin\theta\,e_1+\cos\theta\,e_2)
\]

induces

\[
\psi \rightarrow e^{i\theta}\psi.
\]

Thus the complex polarization coordinate carries
unit charge under the \(U(1)\) frame rotation.

\subsection{Helicity operator}

Consider a rotation around the propagation direction

\[
R_{\hat{\mathbf k}}(\phi).
\]

Under this rotation

\[
e_1 \rightarrow
\cos\phi\, e_1 + \sin\phi\, e_2
\]

\[
e_2 \rightarrow
-\sin\phi\, e_1 + \cos\phi\, e_2
\]

and therefore

\[
\psi \rightarrow e^{i\phi}\psi.
\]

Hence

\[
J_{\hat{\mathbf k}} \psi = +1 \psi.
\]

Similarly

\[
\psi^* \rightarrow e^{-i\phi}\psi^*,
\]

so

\[
J_{\hat{\mathbf k}} \psi^* = -1 \psi^*.
\]

\subsection{Helicity eigenstates}

The circular polarization states are therefore

\[
\psi_+ = \psi
\]

\[
\psi_- = \psi^* .
\]

These satisfy

\[
J_{\hat{\mathbf k}} \psi_\pm
=
\pm \psi_\pm .
\]

Thus the two transverse modes correspond to

\[
h=\pm1.
\]

\subsection{Theorem}

\paragraph{Theorem (Emergent helicity).}

The transverse polarization bundle of the condensed
TECT elastic medium produces two helicity eigenstates

\[
h=+1,
\qquad
h=-1.
\]

These correspond to the two circular polarization modes.

\subsection{Interpretation}

This helicity structure is identical to that of the
Maxwell photon.

The appearance of helicity \(\pm1\) follows purely from

\begin{enumerate}

\item the transversality condition

\[
\mathbf k\cdot \mathbf u =0,
\]

\item the local \(SO(2)\) rotation symmetry of the
transverse frame.

\end{enumerate}

Thus the spin-1 helicity structure emerges geometrically
from the transverse bundle.

% elastic -> maxwell action
